{"name":"Novella 2 — Chapter 1: Narrowvale Pilot","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 1: Narrowvale Pilot\n\nJhace comes in with the rain. Narrowvale’s three-lane choke gleams slick, flags the color of old brass slapping on their cords. Steam from kettles shelves low, the smell of onion and wet rope. He lifts the placard kit to the posting wall and sets his boot on the bottom rung of a bent ladder that doesn’t trust him. The board he needs to cover is crowded: last‑season tax notice, a chipped fish‑day prayer tile, someone’s hand‑ink arrows pointing nowhere.\n\n“Two hands,” he says, not loud, only for the crew near. Kylar anchors the rung with a shin and his palm flat. Brighlee waits at the side with the bell card and an oilcloth roll of ties.\n\nJhace measures with fingers first. Two knuckles from the bolt head, three from the seam in the stone. The Map‑as‑Set board will live here or it will die here; there’s no point being precious somewhere else. He chalks the rectangle, an honest outline that catches the spit of weather, then opens the kit.\n\nIcon plates in their slots: droplet, wedge, ring, arrows, bell marks. The Conclave stamp sits in its felt pocket, weighty as a small vise. Ridger tool wrapped in cloth, teeth fine as a fishbone comb. He checks the ink’s drag with a thumb. Viscosity good; rain won’t thin it into lies.\n\nThe steward arrives like a brass hinge grinding dry. Jall Var carries a ledger in two hands as if it is a baby that might curse. He takes in the chalk, the board, the crew. “Not text,” he says, meaning to cut the thing short. He points with the ledger’s corner. “This lane reads. We post words.”\n\n“Bell windows are color,” Brighlee murmurs, looking nowhere.\n\nJhace gives the steward the polite weight of a glance and no more. The lane is already crowding. Carts at his flank drip, mule ears flick. Vendors fold tarps, then unfold them again when the gust shifts. Authority noise rides the rain: a council runner up the back way, someone calling about a missed fee, three men rehearsing an argument so it feels practiced. The choke is hot metal without air.\n\nHe marks the first anchor. The hammer shows its clean face, and Kylar’s small breath answers each tack with a ready tie. The board goes up steady, strip by strip, not a show. Jhace steps off the ladder, calves buzzing, and wipes rain off the board with the inside of his sleeve. The kit’s latch ticks open again. He slides out the plate for the lane map and lays it to the wood.\n\nMap‑as‑Set replaces words. He sets the three arrows for the three streams of traffic in the choke: left, heavy carts; center, foot and hand‑barrows; right, return flow and runners. The arrows’ tails meet in a simple tooth that suggests the pinch point. He presses ink and pulls clean. The black sits proud without shine. Water beads gather, refuse to smudge, and roll.\n\n“Those are lines,” Jall Var says. “Not law.”\n\nJhace checks the spacing to the caller post he chalked earlier. Brighlee is already there, tying a small square to the post that announces bell windows. Blue for Four, yellow for Two, the squares clipped to spin when the wind wants them. She gives him a look that says: simple holds.\n\n“Law is posted,” Jhace says, soft. He doesn’t say it to the steward; he says it to the board. He fits the ring glyph at waist height, the one that marks the home line for hands. The ring must be obvious but not intrusive. Too high and people miss it; too low and children worry it like a toy. He measures from his belt to the board, sets the ring, inked and honest.\n\nThe wedge glyph sits where it must sit, upstream of a Veil zone that will arrive in two chapters’ worth of work for other people. For now, the wedge simply points to the corner turn standard ahead, a promise the board makes and the crews must keep. He places the wedge and pulls the plate. Clean edge, no feathering. Rain tracks around it like water around a polished stone.\n\nJall Var is not done. “Without text, my clerks must interpret. You invite mishandling. Fines fall wrong. You will cause mistakes.” A vendor nearby, hearing fines, goes quiet. The rain doesn’t.\n\nKylar keeps his hands busy at the ties, learning the icons by touch as he has been taught: arrow is a long bone, wedge is a tooth, ring is a smooth coin, droplet is a tear that points down to where oils belong. Jhace approves the habit by not stopping it. He sets the bell marks in a neat row along the board’s bottom edge, two short stripes, one long, for the cadence that will regulate changes. He hears the future taps in his bones, a steadying thought in the weather’s noise.\n\nHe drifts the ridger out of its cloth and holds it near the bottom right of the card. Tempermark isn’t the ritual today, but a ridge at the legend will keep counterfeits from slipping in early. He presses the teeth with a palm heel until they bite a relief into the inked field. Not a flourish, just a fact you can feel.\n\n“Your people can leave the words to the court?” Jall Var asks, voice climbing. “We are literate. We will not be reduced.”\n\nJhace’s voice is tired from the morning’s pressline run across Grindheim’s span. It comes out hoarse, the way a hinge sounds after salt air. “The corridor reads the icons,” he says. “Clerks can read as much as they like elsewhere.” He lifts the Conclave stamp and sets it under the wedge. The stamp’s face is a small square with the quill mark. He inks it and presses until the wood says yes with a small change in tone. When he pulls away, the black square sits like a seal on a crate. Lawful enough for any steward who bothers to look.\n\n“Who signed that?” Jall Var asks.\n\n“Corlexi,” Brighlee answers, because she was there. She taps the ledger strip that lives in her roll where the approval number sits dry and precise. She doesn’t offer it to the steward. Rain and paper are enemies; this crowd will see it later when it can be seen.\n\nThe choke flexes. A mule pushes its shoulder into the wrong lane. Jhace steps into the center without fanfare, one palm out for gentle stop, one finger to the new map, then a pivot of the wrist that returns the animal to left flow. He doesn’t shout. He doesn’t need to. The driver reads the arrows and yields. Rain runs off the brim of the man’s hat and finds its way to the gutters instead of down the corridor’s spine.\n\nPressure builds again at the back as a cart’s wheel catches on a loose cobble. The sound is a dull metal waver. Jhace feels his calves complain as he half‑crouches to reset the stone with his foot and a pry of the hammer’s handle. Kylar is there with a wedge of wood to keep the repair honest. They don’t exchange words. The work is a unit of actions that fit without comment.\n\n“Card needs warnings,” the steward says, raising his voice to cover the crowd. “Words about penalties. Words about exceptions.” He enjoys the shape of exceptions.\n\nJhace sets the final icon: the droplet. A small mark at the board’s lower left that directs oil use to the annex and keeps the corridor free of false cures. Salee will love it when she sees it. He cleans the plate on the cloth’s dry edge and racks it home. Cost shows in the small shake at his wrist as he unwinds from the ladder one more time. He is thirsty. He is nearly done.\n\nHe steps back until his shoulders touch the posting wall. Brighlee lifts the caller square and flicks Blue to face the lane. “Bell Four weather,” she says, low, so the world can arrange itself. She ties it off with a knot that can be changed with wet fingers. Kylar rechecks the ties in sequence, then palms the ring glyph to test its height, an apprentice ritual they made up in a dry moment and kept.\n\nJall Var opens his mouth, maybe to announce a stay. A gust blows his ledger’s pages into his face, and he takes a small step back. The crowd laughs, the soft kind that makes the temperature of a place rise half a degree.\n\nJhace takes the moment. He lifts the ridger once more and bites a thin ridge along the legend line, the place where a thumb will go if the eyes don’t trust yet. He breathes the way he teaches: in for four, out for six. The breath burns at the top of his chest where the morning left a rasp. He can feel the ache ripening in his calves, a clean soreness like metal cooling right.\n\n“Post is live,” he says to the board. To the lane. Not to the steward. He puts the ridger away, seals the kit, and lets the tools rest.\n\nBehavior changes first at the edges. A barrower reaches the choke and pauses, uncertain, then sees his lane in the arrow and steps into it without being told. A boy with a coil of cord at his waist drifts his hand to the ring glyph as he walks and smiles like something fit in his head without pain. To the right, a pair of runners align themselves and stop fighting space they do not own.\n\nThe steward tries one last time. “If someone asks, where do they read the clause?”\n\nJhace tips his chin at the Conclave square. “There,” he says. “The clause is that this is the clause.” He doesn’t wait for the answer. He turns to the board and rubs the lower corner with the pad of his thumb to see if the ridge holds under rain and flesh. It does.\n\nBrighlee unrolls a small strip with the caller symbol and slips it over the post so the next crew knows where to stand and when. Kylar moves through the first three carts like he was born a knot‑opener, touching nothing, only pointing. A mule snorts, a wheel creaks, steam lifts. Air finds a path again. The corridor’s tone drops half a note.\n\nJhace steps away, hoarseness a scratch that won’t drink water until the work is fully done. He lets the lane show him if it learned something. It did. He feels it in the way the pressure at the choke goes from thrust to glide.\n\nHe sets the stamp again just once, a second bite beside the first, not required, only present for the people who count such things. Then he does the last check: he runs his own thumb over the little Tempermark ridge where the legend lives, skin finding the raised line by instinct rather than sight.\n\nA woman with a market cart does the same, tentative, then sure. Her thumb finds the ridge. The cart eases forward without a shouted order."}

{"name":"Novella 2 — Chapter 2: South Annex Veils","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 2: South Annex Veils\n\nTiffani had the benches warm and the lamps turned down to a honey dim so the glass would tell the truth. The South Clinic Annex breathed its own weather: oil smell, thread kits lined like small bones, veils stacked in rolls that caught the light at their edges. She chalked the wedge glyph on the teaching board, angled toward the corner work, and set the metronome bell two beats soft.\n\nThe hot racks arrived off schedule, metal shrieking against metal. A runner and his partner came shouldering through the Annex arch with the first frame still giving off heat. Steam lifted when rain from their coats met the rack’s bars. Salee swore in the doorway, one clipped syllable, then claimed the oil shelf from a curious trainee with a wrist tap that was gentle and absolute.\n\n“Wedge before Veil,” Tiffani said. She kept her voice low and clean, the way you speak beside reactive material. “Corners three‑count. We practice with dead panes first.” She nodded toward the practice frames, the ones with score marks to show where old failures began.\n\nKylar stood at the edge with a coil of ties he didn’t need for this class but didn’t put down because hands need a task. Valaryn, sleeves rolled, laid out return‑to‑work slips where trainees could see them and not touch. Tiffani let their competence be background muscle. The work in front of her was three apprentices new to Annex discipline and one old hand who had learned in a shop with more shouting than standard.\n\nShe hooked the first dead pane into the frame and traced the route with the raw edge of a wedge plate. The wedge glyph points, the wedge tool speaks; the two must agree. “The wedge is not decoration,” she said. “It is the turn. You set it before you lay a veil. If you veil a live corner without a wedge, the crack you earn is your fault. This will not be argued.”\n\nThe metronome bell ticked twice, soft as fingernails on a jar. She let the sound land in the room’s nerves.\n\n“Three‑count corners,” she said. “Listen first.” She held the corner of the dead pane under the lamp and breathed on it until mist printed. The edge haloed. “One to find the heat,” she counted, finger following the inner edge. “Two to move it.” Her hand drew a small circle with the wedge’s flat. “Three to hold and leave.” She stepped away and the mist thinned to nothing, a clean matte that said, safe.\n\nThe first trainee, a boy whose hands were too strong for their own good, reached for the veil roll too fast. Tiffani took his wrist, not a correction that hurt, only an anchor. “Wedge first,” she said. He switched tools, the small sting of embarrassment serving as a better primer than talk.\n\nThe hot rack thudded into its notch. Heat hummed through the bench, a tone that lived in the steel more than the air. Off schedule meant the glass would come through before she had finished training speed on cold material. She moved the metronome a hair faster with a knuckle. “We adjust but do not skip,” she said to no one and everyone.\n\nSalee set the oil vials above the benches, purity seals intact and obvious. A neat square of color sat on the shelf edge to match the lot’s true hue. Tiffani eyed it once and looked away. Oils lied when people lied; today they would not.\n\nTrainee Two ran her wedge along the edge, angle wrong, pressing too hard. The sound told on her, a dry scrape where the metal should sing. “Listen,” Tiffani said. She took the wedge and made the same pass, a small correction of wrist. The sound shifted to a whisper that lived in the room’s bones. “If it scratches your head, it’s wrong. If it makes you breathe out without knowing, it’s right.”\n\nValaryn cleared her throat softly, not to speak, but to draw a pair of watchers to the posted slips. Paid rest, return‐to‐work: the clinic’s part of Veinwork, visible, not a rumor. Tiffani caught the gesture’s neatness with a brief nod and returned to the bench.\n\n“Corners,” she said. “Three counts.” She placed a fresh pane on the jig. The lamp showed the corner’s gloss a little like ice. “You will want to drag the heat,” she said. “You will not. You will ask it to sit.” She breathed across the corner again and traced the ring where mist laid. “One. Two. Three.” On three she lifted the wedge and set the veil, not touching the bright edge with bare skin, not clouding the flux with damp.\n\nThe old hand leaned forward. “I was taught to veil first,” he muttered, like a confession a person offers right before he decides to ignore you. Tiffani did not look up.\n\n“You were taught wrong,” she said, not unkind. “Your panes failed at the corners and you blamed weather. We have weather every day.” She tilted the frame so the light told the truth. “The wedge makes the corner a decision. The veil protects that decision. That is the order.”\n\nThe second rack arrived out of breath, carried by a team that should have been somewhere else on the clock. Steam again. The sound of wet canvas slapping a wall. Tiffani’s back tightened from hours of leaning into benches across the day; something in her lower spine ticked like a cooling plate. She drank a mouthful from the tin at her hip, the water metallic from the tap and good.\n\nShe moved the class to live glass because time had said so. The hot frame was a patient that would not wait its turn and would punish vanity. “Hands washed, gloves dry,” she said. “If your breath wobbles, step away and call.” No one wobbled. The room was made of people who hated to be the cause.\n\nShe nodded to Trainee Three, the quiet one who had placed her tools in a line and would not start without being told. “Set,” Tiffani said. The wedge went down. The angle was true, the pressure light. She watched the girl hear the room, saw the small slackening in her mouth when the wedge sang across the glass correctly for the first time.\n\n“Corner,” Tiffani said. “Count like you mean it.” The metronome bell ticked. One. Two. Three. The trainee’s breath synced without drama, the body learning before the mind wrote anything down.\n\nThe veil came next, laid with the roll’s leader aligned to the wedge’s exit. No chatter, no flare. The edge took matte like frost receding under sun. Salee, satisfied that no one would waste oil or knock a vial, turned and posted a small reminder square above the shelf: one drop icon, black; a wedge; a quill. Inventory truth is policy.\n\nThe old hand tried to move faster to prove he could. He caught the corner driver just enough to smudge gloss. Tiffani’s palm met his on the tool and took it away. “Stop,” she said. Not a scold. A door closing. “Again. The wedge listens first. Your hands do not outrun heat.”\n\nHe swallowed and stepped back into the beat like a person finding the right place in a song that was already going. His second attempt arrived in the room as quiet as a decent apology. The wedge whispered. The corner held. The veil did not lie.\n\nTiffani felt the day in her back like a wire pulled too tight. She shifted her stance and let weight ride through her heels into the floor. The Annex’s stone took it and gave her steadiness back. She moved bench to bench, checking. Thumb above a corner to call mist, then letting it fade to judge rate. Palm near a seam to sense radial heat. Breath in, count, breath out. “Again,” she said when again was what was required. “Good,” she said once when it would teach better than silence.\n\nThe door admitted a gust that made the lamp flame lean. Air skittered across a finished edge and left it unmoved. Tiffani watched for counterfeit behavior and saw none: inks right, ridges true, oil smell clean. She waved Kylar to the posting wall with the teaching card blank. He held the ties but didn’t tie. That was her part.\n\n“Last pane,” she said. The room had the neat hunger of people about to finish well. She took the tools herself, not to prove anything, only because it was faster and time was a material there was ever less of. Wedge set. Angle clean. The wedge plate kissed the edge like metal speaking to glass in a language both respected. Corner: one, two, three. Veil laid with the leader aligned as if a line drawn on air. The lamp’s cone showed the gloss give up its shine and settle.\n\nShe nodded once, enough for the room to release the held breath. Back complained, mouth dry, hands steady.\n\nAt the wall, she inked the teaching card: wedge glyph large and honest at top left, three short bars stacked beside it to mark the counts, a small ring glyph at waist height to remind of home line even here where benches and bodies could crowd truth out. Bell marks in the lower field for the cadence they practiced. Conclave square with quill stamped in the corner, authority quiet and visible. The ridger tool left a fine bite across the legend strip so fingers could tell if the board was real.\n\nShe tied the card to the board at the Annex’s end, knots set so a wet hand could change them. Kylar checked the ties in sequence with a fingertip, reading the icons like raised letters, then stepped back.\n\nThe class drifted close. No one touched the card. They didn’t have to. The lamps hummed in their cages. Tiffani leaned over the last pane and breathed one more time across the corner. The gloss surrendered. Matte held. In that soft light the edge looked like it had exhaled. She turned her head and the lamplight ran clean along the line.\n\nOn the posted card, the wedge glyph sat clear as a coin. Beneath it, in that same honey dim, the pane’s edge showed safe matte while the brief mist thinned and was gone."}

{"name":"Novella 2 — Chapter 3: Pressline Cadence","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 3: Pressline Cadence\n\nJhace reached the Narrowvale choke ahead of the bell change and found the weather already doing the work of a poor foreman. Rain hit in sheets that turned cobbles to polished iron. Kettle steam bent under the gusts and laid a low fog at shin height. The lane held like a frayed rope in a hand that hadn’t decided to let go or pull. Bell Two’s window was about to end; Bell Four would raise its face. That seam was where lanes came apart.\n\nHe slung the short bar across his shoulder. It was nothing more than a length of tempered rod with a coin‑thick cap for the taps. The pressline lives in small, clear sounds. He kept the placard kit under his cloak to keep the ridger teeth dry. Brighlee stood already at the caller post, Blue square still showing, fingers at the tie knot, eyes on him. Kylar held a coil of cord and the little hammer like a young man who had understood that hands idle at a choke were a liability.\n\nVendors shoved for last sales before a window shift. A man with red sleeves decided the center lane was his private invention and angled a cart into foot traffic. A girl carrying oil tins bumped a shoulder and swallowed her pain so it wouldn’t leak. Authority noise gathered on the edges like a storm building its own weather. The bell rope twitched down the corridor where it hung from its housing.\n\nJhace set his palm on the caller post to feel the lane’s tone through the wood, the way a bridgewright learns to listen to his spans. The energy had turned granular; there was no flow, only a crowd of particles insulting each other. He took a breath that hurt a little from the morning’s chill and let it out slow. Then he lifted the bar.\n\nTwo short taps.\n\nThey landed clean, coin on rod, a sound with borders. The first found the crowd’s ears. The second told them the first wasn’t a mistake. He waited a breath and delivered them again. Two short taps. He kept the spacing with his ribs, not his head.\n\nBrighlee flipped the caller square from Blue to Yellow with a quick twist and tied it so it could be cut fast if weather asked. Kylar drifted along the left boundary, pointing the front axle of Red Sleeves back to the heavy lane with a patient hand and the plain authority of someone who belonged to the board.\n\nA vendor near the steam carts shoved the man in front of him to prove a point to a third party. The shove shook the middle lane like a wire. Jhace did not look there. He looked at the heads down the way and counted four, then six, that had started to move in sympathy with the taps. Two short. Two short. The cadence did not tell them what to do; it taught them when to do what the board already asked.\n\nHe used his left hand for the bar, the one that took its orders more reluctantly, to preserve the right for fine work if he needed it. The weather’s needles pushed into the cuff of his sleeve. The rod’s cap rang true; the sound bounced once off the stone face and returned corrected. He saw the first knot slacken where runners had tangled with a barrow and tried to share space.\n\nThe bell in its housing made up its mind and let go of Two. The last notes hung. People habitually surge at the seam, wanting to capture an advantage that doesn’t exist. He sent the taps again, a hair faster, still paired. Two short. Two short. The pressline is not a marshal. It’s a metronome you can feel in your ankle bones.\n\nRed Sleeves caught his eye, not with a challenge, only because he had been forced to notice. Jhace touched the map‑as‑set board with the rod’s cap to point and then lowered the rod and tapped. Tap tap. The driver’s chin dipped once. He backed his wheel an inch and the jam that had been a problem turned into a problem that had edges. Edge problems you can work.\n\nThe lane breathed. Drops hit puddles in complex polyrhythms until they started to organize themselves around the tapping like iron filings finding a field. He felt the odd ache begin in his right wrist anyway, the cost of a day that had been more arm than leg. It would be a dull throb by night, the sort you soak out with heat and small patience. He kept the left hand working and let the right hang loose at his side.\n\nBrighlee called nothing. She didn’t need to. The cadence carried the message that the window had turned and the rules of the board continued. She held up two fingers toward the bell housing for the ringer to see through the steam. The ringer answered with a nod that didn’t care if anyone else witnessed it. Work acknowledging work.\n\nSomewhere behind the soup stall, a barked complaint rose, tried to find allies, failed, and ran out of energy against the taps. Two short. Two short. Jhace moved three paces toward the center and touched the map with his thumb at the corner where earlier he had set the wedge glyph. There, the knife‑point of the choke where flow decided whether the corridor was a lane or a fight. He tapped once against the board as a courtesy to his own method and stepped back to hear what would happen without him.\n\nIt happened. The knot by the barrow opened at the edges first. A wheel nudged into a space that had not been space thirty seconds ago. A child holding a depth of cloth recognized that the pressline was older than his inclination to dart. He waited. Two short. Two short. He moved during the spaces and the moment he chose belonged to him because the corridor had lent it.\n\nKylar made his loop at the front, the runner’s light step that touches every square of the problem without owning any of it. He set the little hammer’s face lightly to a tie that had sagged at the caller post and took the slack out without ceremony. The young have gifts; this one’s was reading icons by finger in the rain, correcting small things before they borrowed money from big things.\n\nThe shove from the vendor line tried to become a practice. Jhace put three beats of silence between tap sets and watched the shove fail in the quiet because there was no rhythm it could break. Then he resumed. Tap tap. Tap tap. His calves remembered last week’s climb and protested at the stance he kept that balanced weight across slick stone. He shifted, preserved his hinge, and let that be the adjustment.\n\nRed Sleeves put his palm briefly to the ring glyph at the map’s waist height as he passed it, the unconscious confession of a man who had been angry and now wasn’t sure at what. The ring’s paint had been polished by many such hands already this week. It gleamed even under rain, a small truth in a wet world.\n\nWhen the surge was ninety percent solved, which is the number that lets a place heal itself the rest of the way, Jhace lowered the rod and let the cadence live in the crowd for a few breaths without him. It did. He felt unnecessary, which is how a standard should make the person who sets it feel on a good day.\n\nHe turned to the board. The Map‑as‑Set carried the arrows, ring, wedge, and the bell marks he’d placed earlier. Today needed words without words: a clause in icon language that taught the taps to outlive the man who held the rod. He opened the kit under his cloak and kept the inner lining between the ridger and the rain.\n\nThe clause was a strip that would live at the board’s lower field: two short bars paired, then a small gap, then the pair again, with a quill‑square to show Conclave authority. He set the plate, checked the spacing with a thumb, and inked with care. The first pull sat true. The second did too. He breathed. His wrist complained when he forced the ridger to bite the legend line’s edge so the strip couldn’t be lifted clean without telling on itself. He accepted the cost and kept going.\n\nBrighlee stepped close and tilted her oilcloth so his work stayed honest. She did not speak. Kylar kept the crowd’s edge from licking at the posting space by simply existing where he existed, a body that said the board was not a negotiable surface.\n\nJhace lifted the stamp and set the small Conclave square with the quill mark at the clause’s right margin. He pressed until the wood changed tone under his palm and then he released. The square sat with the comfortable authority of a pin that had been set cooler than birth. He didn’t need a witness for this. The witness would be behavior.\n\nHe tapped the rod once against the post to clear water and set cadence again, this time standing in front of the new clause strip as if it had always been there. Tap tap. Tap tap. He matched his sound to what the board now declared.\n\nA vendor near the edge who loved exceptions more than food opened his mouth and then closed it because his feet had already obeyed. The ringer at the housing let the first of Bell Four’s notes out. They rode over the tapping rather than against it. Rain smacked the ground like small coins. The pressline held.\n\nJhace finished the last pass with the rod when the window’s seam had fully laid down. He let the bar rest against his shoulder and rolled his right wrist with the small grimace you allow when no one is looking, felt the tendon’s heat, and made a private note about wrapping it later with clinic salts.\n\nHe wiped a sleeve across the lower board where water had tried to pool and then stepped back to see the whole like he always did, against the habit of zooming in that had saved him earlier lives and would kill this one if he let it have the reins.\n\nThe clause lived where it should. Two short bars, a measured space, two short bars again. No text about penalties. No room for misreading. Conclave square biting the corner. The ring glyph shone where hands had made it honest. The arrows told their quiet story.\n\nHe let the crowd show him the result. It did. The center lane shifted from personal to communal; the left tightened to a competent squeeze; the right carried return flow without flinching. A runner passed with knees high to keep his shoes from taking in water, hand brushing the ring in passing, eyes already on the next choke.\n\nJhace set the rod down against the post, secured by a simple tie so the next person could find it without hunting. He pressed the ridger once more into the clause edge to leave that fine bite for fingers that asked if it was real.\n\nOn the board beside the arrows, the cadence clause sat newly posted, ink glossy in the rain. At his feet, puddles took the two short taps and answered. Rings spread, matched the beat, and then melted into each other as the knot opened."}

{"name":"Novella 2 — Chapter 4: Counterfeit Placard","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 4: Counterfeit Placard\n\nTiffani met the choke at first bell after rain, when ink tells on itself. The posting wall carried the Map‑as‑Set card Jhace had set, the cadence strip, a clinic notice square from Valaryn, and—new overnight—a Tempermarked caution plate someone had tucked in at the edge like a helpful afterthought. The edge was wrong. The board wanted to be believed too badly.\n\nShe palmed the ridger in her pocket to keep her hand honest and stepped into the spray. Floor hands drifted closer with the look people wear when they suspect a thing and hope to be wrong. A man with damp apron and a printer’s nail bed stood within ear, attention too careful. Forger nearby, she marked, and let the note sink without changing her face.\n\nAction first: she touched the plate. A proper Tempermark gives the thumb something to argue with. This one was smooth, shine like wet glass not ink. Her fingertip skated. Flat.\n\nBrighlee arrived at her shoulder with the ties and the Conclave stamp packet, eyes already on the strangers who had made themselves temporarily busy. Kylar hovered by the caller post, one hand on home line like a boy setting himself to be reliable if called. The lane was thin now, early carts rolling in the clean after-rain light. Good time to pull a lie.\n\nTiffani curled the corner of the suspect plate and checked bite. A real board resists then yields; this peeled without complaint, like a sticker sold to children. She didn’t rip it yet. Public corrections live better if you show the why first.\n\n“Fingers,” she said, not loud, only to the three nearest vendors who’d stopped selling to watch. She held the counterfeit where it could be reached and held up the real board’s legend strip beside it. “Here, then here.” She pressed her own thumb first along the posted clause on the real board, where the ridger’s teeth had laid a fine bite yesterday. The tiny ridge met her skin with the respectful push of a truth refusing to be ignored. She slid to the fake. Smooth. “Feel the difference,” she said.\n\nThey did. First vendor’s face changed before he had words. Second closed his eyes for the test and nodded like a person listening to a song that proves itself in the first bar. Third hissed half a breath through teeth.\n\n“Counter,” Brighlee said low, confirming the call and shifting her stance so her body made a small wall against a sudden wind. The printer’s nail bed man looked at the sky like he admired weather and had a place to be. He didn’t move away. People who had good paper rarely needed their courage to keep them still.\n\nTiffani kept her hand steady. The day had already taxed it with bench work at the Annex; the small muscles along her thumb complained at the fine control. She locked her wrist and let arm bone carry the work.\n\n“Bite pattern,” she said, for the floor hands to hear. She lifted the corner again and showed the back. The adhesive spread was too clean, the fiber too thin, the ink an unconvincing black that had gone gray in the wet. She set the counterfeit down on the board’s shelf and took the ridger out where anyone could see it. “Tempermark is a ridge made with teeth,” she said. “Ink sits proud. Water beads and walks off. Counterfeits share shine and lie flat.”\n\nShe pressed the ridger into the fake’s lower edge. Teeth left a surface scratch and no ridge. The tool’s voice on the material was wrong, a papery squeak instead of the soft cut of ink and wood agreeing. She let the sound travel.\n\nA murmuring started in the ring of watchers like a kettle before boil. Floor hands, anxious, wanted her to name the forger. She didn’t. Standards bite the object, not the man, until the board makes it necessary to write names. She set the counterfeit’s corner back down with care, then lifted it clean and tore it along the field so the false ridge could not be repurposed. The tear showed fiber that belonged to cheaper paper than any Conclave stock. She held the torn piece beside the real board one last time and felt both with two thumbs to anchor the lesson in skin.\n\nKylar stepped in with the cord and pinned the safe side of the posting wall so a gust wouldn’t turn the work into a sail. Brighlee laid a dry oilcloth on the shelf and opened the stamp packet. The Conclave quill square sat in the felt as if it had no opinions beyond gravity.\n\nThe printer’s nail bed man shifted his weight, then tried a small smile at the vendor next to him as if to be included in a safer story. The vendor did not offer him one.\n\n“Posting a warning square,” Tiffani said. She took the spare card from the kit: a small field with no text, only a ridged legend strip and the quill square space. She set it to the wall where the counterfeit had been and pressed two ties through with Kylar’s help. The board accepted its new inhabitant with the same bored dignity as stone accepts rain.\n\nShe inked the quill square and pressed. The wood changed tone under her palm. She lifted and checked for clean bite. Good. She set the ridger to the legend strip and pressed a fine ridge the width of a thumbprint. The teeth hummed. Raised ink stood like a tiny levee that water could admire but not conquer. She turned the card sideways and pressed a second bite to form a small crosshatch patch in one corner—an agreed place for the public to test without destroying the card’s working surface.\n\nBrighlee took the torn counterfeit piece and pinned it lower on the wall under the warning square, a museum of dishonesty. She set a small red thread through it, not recall slash—this wasn’t a lot code matter—but a visual cue that strangers would read as wrong. The Conclave had allowed that tiny thread for this exact purpose on Corridor cards. Not a penalty, a lesson.\n\n“Why not fine him,” a floor hand asked, unable to keep the question in his chest. Eyes ticked toward the printer. The man’s jaw lay loose, too careful, listening for the exact shape of his name in public air.\n\n“Because we fix the corridor first,” Tiffani said, and let the rest be obvious and later. She set the fake’s remaining piece into the burn tray Kylar produced and shook a pinch of drying sand over it to keep ink from wandering. “And because the next one watching will stop if the ridge stops them.”\n\nShe lifted the warning square’s edge and set the small Conclave serial number at bottom right. The stamp bit clean again. She did not look up to meet the steward’s eye because the steward hadn’t earned it yet today.\n\nThe printer twitched toward departure. Brighlee, casual as a rain gutter, flowed to occupy the air in front of him so his first step would be a collision. He reconsidered and stayed. The lane watched itself, which is a pleasure when it happens.\n\nTiffani’s hand shook once, a fine tremor from the steady‑hand taxes of the Annex and now this. She flattened it by laying it over the posted card’s ridged patch until her skin taught itself again what true felt like. Breath in, four. Out, six. The tremor listened.\n\nValaryn’s return‑to‑work slips fluttered in a breeze at the wall’s far end and settled. The lane carried its own business for a minute without asking anyone’s help. The steam carts hissed. The ring glyph at waist height had a new polish where people touched it to think.\n\nTiffani let the crowd see the sequence again in two motions: thumb to real ridge, thumb to counterfeit’s flat. She held still so they could memorize the difference by watching her skin behave. Then she tipped the burn tray and fed the torn counterfeit to a small dish flame the clinic used for sample sterilizations. Paper darkened, curled, and failed. Ink turned to a thin smoke that the rain pushed down and away.\n\n“Posted,” Brighlee said, tying the last tie of the warning square so a wet hand could change it later if it needed moving. Kylar checked the ties in order because that’s what he does when nervous: he lets sequence save him.\n\nThe printer breathed like a man adjusting his story and stepped back half a pace. No one followed. No one needed to. The board had more authority than anyone’s mouth.\n\nTiffani lifted the ridger again and pressed a small second bite at the warning card’s lower edge as if she were signing with a line only fingers can read. She tucked the tool away and rolled her shoulders once. The cost would arrive later as long lines in her hand that only heat and rest could flatten. For now, the room’s nerves had calmed one notch.\n\nA boy with a cord at his waist reached out with theatrical caution and touched the crosshatch patch, face serious. He looked up at her and she nodded. He did it again, lighter, then pressed the flat of the burned scrap’s cooled ash against his fingertip and found nothing to feel. He said nothing. He didn’t need to.\n\nThe forger‑adjacent man made himself small and slid toward the edge where crowds dissolve. He would be spoken to later, not here, not with weather watching. This moment belonged to the board.\n\nTiffani looked once more along the wall. Map‑as‑Set, cadence clause, clinic square, and now the warning. She stepped back until rain traced the rim of her hood and opened her palm to the two surfaces that mattered.\n\nOn the left, the posted warning square stood with its ridged patch proud, droplets beading and lifting. On the right, between her fingers, the last unburned scrap of counterfeit showed ink that lay flat and dead. She ran a fingertip across both to teach feeling to strangers who might watch. One surface pushed back like good steel under cloth. The other offered nothing at all."}

{"name":"Novella 2 — Chapter 5: Countermark Dispute (Yard)","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 5: Countermark Dispute (Yard)\n\nJhace stepped into the Reclaim Yard with wet shoulders and the taste of iron in his teeth. Pins and plates leaned in ranks against the wall like a graveyard of decisions. A crowd held to the rain shadow of a bent canopy, watching a single lane pin clamped to the public anvil. The pin wore a mark that had no business on a working corridor: a proud flourish stamped deep and glossy, wider than standard, the ego‑tooth of a legacy brast.\n\nKylar stood at the stanchion with the countermark bundle cradled in his arms as if it might try to climb away. He’d posted the small “inspection” square on the yard board, quill‑boxed and dry. Brighlee, wind‑chapped, had brought the witness ledger and was taking the weather’s measure with one eye and the crowd’s with the other.\n\nThe legacy brast himself waited with chin forward, coat open to make sure everyone saw the mark he’d carried through three owners and a flood. The set on the pin had been decent in its day, but the bite pattern had lied about what was safe. The map updates from Narrowvale demanded a different tooth, cooler, narrower, truer.\n\n“It’s mine,” the man said, not loud, not offering it for debate. Pride spoken like a stamped letter.\n\nJhace didn’t answer him. He set his palm on the pin’s neck and felt the cold in its core. He watched a bead of water sit and not travel. He bent close and looked at the mark’s shoulder where the bite should have feathered to guide force. It ended in a flashy shoulder instead, a place where stress would start a hairline and then argue its way into a crack at the worst time.\n\nHe nodded once to himself and reached for the countermark slip on the board’s peg. Thin paper stiffened with wax at the edges so rain would not unmake procedure. He set it on the anvil’s flat with a coin for weight and inked the reason code: **R‑3: Legacy ego‑mark nonstandard; unsafe shoulder.** His handwriting was blocky from cold. He rubbed his knuckles and set the range box: **cooler‑than‑birth: 120–140** with a small tick mark at **131**, the number he expected to like today.\n\nKylar fed the small brazier at the anvil two thumb‑thick sticks and coaxed a tight bloom of heat. No white quench anywhere near. No overbind. This was a language of degrees and patience. Jhace lifted the pin with the tongs and set its mark face toward the glow, not close enough to bully the metal, just near enough to teach it to forget.\n\nHe felt the yard gather behind him. People like to watch obsolete pride bend. He didn’t mind being their instrument as long as they learned the standard and not the story. Brighlee turned the witness ledger to a dry page and tucked a strip of oilcloth under the bottom edge so the rain could pretend to be helpful elsewhere.\n\nJhace held a sliver of temper wax to the pin’s shoulder. The wax softened at a pace he trusted. Cooler than birth, close enough to memory to let the metal admit correction without screaming. He checked the angle twice, then a third time, and let the heat run in one clean breath along the mark. The old shine went cloudy at the edge like breath on glass.\n\nThe legacy brast stepped nearer, the audience narrowing itself to one face. “You erase me,” he said. It wasn’t a question. The words had the hunger of a man who expected to be fought.\n\n“You lied about shoulder,” Jhace said, not unkind. “The corridor pays for that. Not today.” He took the set tool from the kit, the one with the honest Conclave tooth, cooler than birth and certified. He showed the tooth to the air and then to the crowd. “Witness,” he said to Brighlee.\n\nShe stepped to the anvil’s other side, ledger open. Kylar moved to have eyes on the pin’s back and the brazier both. The yard’s noise dimmed.\n\nJhace set the tooth, checked the alignment by feel rather than sight, and gave the mark three taps that were not for cadence, only for forming. The sound went into the metal and did not return confused. He lifted, wiped moisture from his eyebrow with the back of his wrist, and set again for the feather. Tap. Tap. He rotated the tool a degree and breathed in. He held it there and breathed out, ceding heat to air.\n\nThe old flourish began to fail under the new bite, not vanishing, but lowered from command to memory. The glossy shoulder dulled to a working face. The tooth seats clean when you get the range right; it sits like it has been waiting.\n\nKylar touched the pin’s cool side and nodded to himself, an apprentice’s private book adding a line he would carry. The crowd rustled. Someone at the back changed their mind about saying something clever and let the moment keep its adult quiet.\n\nJhace checked the wax again. It touched 132 and then relaxed. He set the tool for the final bite, a correction line that would refuse to accept the old shoulder’s lie if a tired crew in rain tried to repeat it later. He pressed, not with strength but with stubborn geometry. Tap. He lifted.\n\nHe let the metal talk to him through the tongs. The new mark sat honest. Not pretty, never pretty. He took the light from the brazier with his hand and moved it across the face to see how the sheen broke. The old gloss had become a soft dull like storm water settling in a pan. The bite remained like a notch in a bar you check with your fingernail each time because you respect it.\n\nThe legacy brast’s throat worked. He didn’t say sorry; a man like that spends apologies like gold and usually never has any in his pocket. He did drop his chin an inch in a human acknowledgment of a standard older than any single person. It was enough.\n\n“Consent,” Brighlee said, that one word shaped into a ledger line. She held the slip and the oilcloth edge in a way that made it impossible to pretend you didn’t hear.\n\nJhace set the pin down on the anvil’s wood and took the quill. He signed in the setter line, hand cramping, letters honest enough to be recognized in bad light. He set the cooling range box again with a dot and wrote **tool: Conclave bite A‑2** on the small space for equipment. Brighlee turned the page toward the legacy brast without offering comfort.\n\nThe man looked at the crowd, which looked back the way a span looks at a person who jumps on it to see if it will hold. He took the quill. The nib made a small scratch when it found a fiber in the paper that didn’t love him. He signed under **consent**. Kylar’s mouth didn’t move but his shoulders eased three grains.\n\n“Witness,” Brighlee said. She signed the witness line with her travel‑small hand, tight and neat, then set the ledger aside and took out the Conclave serial stamp for the slip. The square bit, inked, in the lower left where rain could not lift it. She pressed the cooling range with a tiny ridge from the ridger teeth so fingers could audit it later.\n\nJhace could feel fatigue now not as a particular pain but as a hollowness behind the ribs. He reached into his pocket for the small clinic salts wrapped in paper and bit off half a square. It woke his mouth with clean bitterness and dragged his focus back to the yard’s plane.\n\nHe lifted the pin again with the tongs and set it on the bench for air to finish what the brazier began. He pressed his thumb to the new bite, then to the dulled shoulder, then held up his hand so the crowd could read something in his skin: metal told truth when asked correctly.\n\nKylar ran to the posting board with the finished slip while Brighlee set the rejected flourish’s fragment—one thin curl of squashed gloss—into the yard’s small burn pan. She touched a match, and the curl darkened and split. No drama. Honest materials accepting end of life.\n\nJhace turned to the legacy brast. “That pin goes back into lane work when Chloen’s maps say its tooth belongs,” he said. The man took it without a word and without the visible shame of a person corrected in public. Maybe the correction had landed on the metal and not on his throat. That’s where it should live.\n\nThe yard exhaled the way steel sings after strain. Tools returned to hooks. The brazier shut its eye. Rain slurred into a steady line instead of a curtain.\n\nJhace wiped the anvil face once out of habit. He washed his hands at the barrel and let the chill make its case. Then he walked to the posting board without hurry.\n\nThe countermark slip hung where it should, ties slanting just enough to shed water. The quill square sat dark. Two names dried under the clause: his under **setter**, Brighlee’s under **witness**, the legacy brast’s under **consent**, precisely where it needed to be for the paper chain to carry weight.\n\nHe touched the lower corner where the ridger had left its fine patch for fingers and found the bite he expected. Beside it, the pin lay on the bench, old sheen dulled to a working face. In that flat light the new tooth caught nothing but purpose. The posted slip took the rain and didn’t blur; the ink held like a coin pressed into a palm and kept."}

{"name":"Novella 2 — Chapter 6: Oil Purity Check","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 6: Oil Purity Check\n\nTiffani reached the South Annex before the bell and found the oil crates stacked like squat bricks against the inner wall, water beading on waxed lids. Salee had chalked a neat box on the bench: samples to test, seals to break, seals to replace. The room carried the good smell of iron racks cooling in another hall and the neutral smell of clean glass. Ration slips fluttered from a nail near the door, three already torn and gone before noon.\n\n“Hot rack in twenty,” Salee said, not a complaint. “We clear these first.” She tapped the top crate with the back of a knuckle. The tap made a small wet thud: heavy enough, not sloshy. She lifted the lid with her thumb on the droplet icon stamped in red, then stopped. “Seal’s wrong,” she said.\n\nTiffani leaned in. The seal color should have been umber to match the lot year. This one leaned two shades light. Under rain, colors lie; under lamps, truth returns. She slid a lamp closer and turned the wick down so the light went gentle. The seal still looked wrong.\n\nPressure crept in from the corridor like heat through a thin wall. Jall Var’s voice ran a lane over, enumerating rations to anyone who needed the theatre. The Annex didn’t need it. Tiffani kept her mouth closed until usefulness was available.\n\nSalee set a shallow dish on the bench and opened the crate anyway, steady. Vials nested in straw, glass shoulders gleaming under wax. Lot code stenciled on the inside lid in a neat hand: **L‑72 / P‑9**. She lifted one vial and rolled it in her fingers to check for bubbles that shouldn’t be there. The oil clung to the glass with a slightly fast retreat. Not proof, not yet.\n\n“We test,” Tiffani said. She took a clean pane from the practice frame bin. It was a square of truth no one could argue with. She wiped it with a lintless cloth and set it on the bench. Salee unsealed the vial with a soft twist that told the wax was brittle. She let one drop fall from the glass lip onto the pane.\n\nViscosity answered in how a drop spreads and breathes. Pure oil sits proud, then relaxes like a quiet lung. Cut oil takes the edge first then hurries into itself. This drop rimmed, hesitated, then rushed an indecisive half‑inch and stopped like a person changing stories mid‑sentence.\n\nTiffani lifted the pane to the lamp and watched the edge move. “Fast,” she said. Salee made a mark in the ledger beside **L‑72**: a small angled slash that meant, watch.\n\nShe set the pane down and took out the oil chalks. They lived in a leather roll wrapped against moisture, three sticks: white for drag, gray for feather, black for flow. She drew a line through the drop with the white chalk. The mark should have parted smooth and left a clean channel. Instead, the chalk stuttered, then broke into a bead at the exit like water pushed where it didn’t belong.\n\n“Again,” Salee said. She liked to see a wrong show itself twice. Tiffani repeated with the gray. The feather at the line’s edge went ragged. Salee set another mark in the ledger, then reached for a second vial from deeper in the crate so a single misfilled bottle wouldn’t call the tune. Different vial, same lot. Wax better. She let a drop fall on fresh glass. The behavior matched: a quick sulk outward, then obstinate stillness.\n\nThe door let in Jall Var and two steps of his impatience. He brought rain with him like it was a credential. “Rations are lean,” he began. His tone expected a discount for urgency. His eyes went to the racks that were not his business and then to the vials that were.\n\nSalee did not look up. She set a third test: heat rise. A low lamp under a small tripod warmed the pane until a breath of shimmer moved in the oil. Pure holds gloss, cut blooms dull. The drop bloomed like a small lie.\n\nTiffani felt her mouth dry. She had walked the lanes since dawn and forgotten to eat. The room tilted one degree and then righted. She cleared her throat and let the sound be the only admission.\n\n“The lot is wrong,” she said. “L‑72 fails viscosity and feather. Seal shade lied.”\n\n“Accept the crate and post caution,” Jall Var said. He aimed for reasonable to hide his speed. “We cannot delay frames for oil. There are returns to work.”\n\n“Tinctures lie on glass,” Salee said, mild. “We don’t post caution on lies.” She moved the vials she had tested into a small metal tray lined with cloth. Each glass neck clicked once against the tray’s rim, a rhythm like coins counting themselves. She set the tray to the far side of the bench, away from clean stock.\n\nTiffani stepped to the posting wall and pulled down the recall bundle. The red slash should be saved for batch recall, but the packet allowed a pre‑slash on an oil lot flagged for removal within the Annex. Not a corridor quarantine yet, just a refusal to let a single bad number touch work. She set the stencil for **L‑72** on the small square and drew a diagonal red line across it with a steady hand. The ink sat like a command. She let it dry.\n\nJall Var tried a different door into the same room. “If we short the Annex now,” he said, “we short the corridor later. Accept half.”\n\nSalee tilted her head toward the purity chart that wasn’t posted yet because Tiffani hadn’t made it into a card in this room. “We post truth and work inside it,” she said to the air. “We don’t carve polite holes.”\n\nThe second crate’s seal matched year. Lot code **L‑73 / P‑9**. Salee broke the wax. The first vial’s drop behaved like good weather: sat proud, relaxed, pulled its edge in slow and quiet. Gray chalk feathered clean. Under heat, the gloss held. She clicked two more vials into the tray of acceptable stock and marked the ledger with a square that meant, fit for Annex use.\n\nTiffani opened the card kit and tugged out a fresh board the size of two hands. She drew a grid of color squares along the top: umber, brick, slate, straw, with a small blank marked **test here**. Under the colors she placed three icons: droplet, quill‑square for Conclave reference, and the clinic’s ⭕ at bottom right. To the side she printed three simple lines: **drag**, **feather**, **flow**, each with a short bar for chalk swipes.\n\nShe pressed the ridger into the legend strip so fingers could learn before eyes agreed. The teeth left a fine ridge a thumb could trust even wet. Salee brought the Conclave stamp for reference and Tiffani set it small, not to borrow authority, only to show the chart matched a published standard. She added Salee’s initials in the lower margin next to **Annex stock control**.\n\nThey worked the remaining vials in order. **L‑72** continued to misbehave. **L‑73** held. A third crate, **L‑68** from older stock, tested true. Tiffani’s stomach pulled tight like wire at the back and then forgot about itself when her hands needed precision. She drank a small mouth from the tin at her hip and let the metallic water wake her mouth and not her hunger.\n\nSalee closed **L‑72** and replaced its lid with the seal reversed, a quiet sign for staff that the crate was not to be touched. She set the red‑slashed square on top so anyone who reached would have to move a refusal first. “Post,” she said.\n\nTiffani walked the card to the wall. She tied it above the vials shelf where people spied it without trying, knots loose enough to adjust and tight enough to ignore weather. She rubbed the ridged patch once, then pressed the clinic ⭕ stamp into the lower right. She left a small blank at the bottom for lot codes to live as a running line of truth.\n\n“Write the lot,” Salee said, already tallying remaining stock. Tiffani inked **L‑73 / P‑9** under **fit** and **L‑68 / P‑3** under **fit**. She set **L‑72 / P‑9** under **rejected** with a small red corner slash. She didn’t slash the corridor cards. That would be Jhace and Chloen tomorrow if they needed to quarantine. Inside the Annex, the mark said enough.\n\nJall Var waited for a noise he could use and heard none. The room made the sound of good work: cloth against glass, lids settled, a lamp turned down. He looked at the chart, at the ledger, at the red square on the crate, and rearranged his face into neutrality. He left with his rain.\n\nValaryn entered with a roll of return‑to‑work slips and saw the red square. She pivoted without commentary to adjust the day’s slips for lower oil usage, posting a small note under the clinic ⭕ that read as a time change rather than a complaint. It would save a future argument no one needed to have.\n\nTiffani felt the cost in her middle, not as pain, just as a lack that pulled her tall. She placed the last of the clean vials in a neat row and stepped back so the room could show her if it had learned anything. It had. No hands drifted toward the reversed‑seal crate. Trainees looked at the chart before asking out loud. Salee’s ledger squared up with the shelf’s new shape.\n\n“Chart reads,” Salee said. She approved with her chin. “Lot lives in the open. We’re not short. We’re honest.” She picked up the ration slips and posted them above the chart in a V shape that made a corridor of paper.\n\nTiffani wiped the pane, cleaned the chalks, rolled them back into their leather, then took the rejected vial from the tray and set it into the burn dish to break the habit of reaching for shiny things. The flame licked the lip; the oil guttered and turned to a thin string of smoke that smelled like nothing at all, which is the smell of a lie ending.\n\nShe let herself lean one shoulder into the cool stone beside the shelf for three breaths. Heat left her hands and went into the wall where it wouldn’t be wasted. The room steadied at a low hum. Outside, a bell note bent and straightened.\n\nShe stepped back to the wall for the last small job. With the ridger, she bit a second patch into the chart’s **test here** square so fingers could compare the ridge to the chalk’s drag. She pressed the Conclave stamp lightly again, not to double it, only to line it up so it would be obvious if anyone tried to lift the chart and replace it.\n\nThe Annex felt correct. Stock updated. Chart posted. Crate red‑slashed. She picked up a clear vial from **L‑73** and held it to the lamp. The liquid inside showed a clean, slow line around its shoulder. Beside it on the lid of **L‑72**, the red‑slashed square flagged the off‑color seal without a speech. The two sat in the same light, one quiet and right, the other refusing to be handled."}

{"name":"Novella 2 — Chapter 7: Batch Recall","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 7: Batch Recall\n\nJhace hit the Narrowvale choke at Bell Two‑to‑Four seam with the recall bundle under his arm and Chloen already chalking the stone. Rain had stopped, but the air still tasted like a cooled pan. On the posting wall, Tiffani’s warning card about counterfeits sat where it should, and Salee’s oil chart was copied small in a corner by someone’s careful hand. L‑72 lived on that chart with a red corner slash. Today, that slash would get teeth.\n\n“Lot and pass,” Chloen said, soft. She had the stencils for **L** and **P** ready, the little quill‑square stamp tucked under her thumb. Kylar uncoiled the quarantine cord like it was a live snake that respected him.\n\nA boy with a hand‑barrow pushed through where he shouldn’t and stopped, reading the arrows late. Vendors muttered about time and fees. Steam curled from a kettle and drew a map of impatience across the lane. Jhace set the recall bundle on the shelf, cracked it open, and found the red slash placards stacked tidy as tiles. He tapped his calf against the post to wake the muscle. Long day ahead.\n\n“Start at south end,” he said. “Red slash on both walls. Cord between. Caller posts swing wide.” His throat still held sand from yesterday’s taps; the rasp lived there like a minor debt.\n\nThey moved. Action inside the first minute or it grows legs. Jhace tied the first red‑slashed placard at the south mouth. **L‑72 / P‑9** stenciled clean. The diagonal slash took authority from ink and gave it to behavior. He pressed the ridger along the legend so fingers could audit in rain. Chloen mirrored him at the north mouth with the same placard, hands quick, face blank in the good way. Kylar ran the cord between posts with a runner’s straight line, then pulled it taut enough to refuse a casual step and loose enough to yield to a stretcher if needed.\n\nA vendor with red sleeves—the same from the cadence day or his cousin—raised his chin. “You going to stop a whole lane because of somebody’s chalk?” His tone tried to recruit allies. The crowd shifted its weight, considering.\n\n“Because of a lot code,” Chloen said, without looking up. She set the quill‑square stamp at the placard’s lower right. The stamp’s bite sang quiet. “Because the charts say so.”\n\n“Charts made by who?” Red Sleeves asked. “Who made you the ones?”\n\nTiffani came in from the Annex side with a coil of secondary ties braced at her hip, rain the color of pewter rimmed on her hood. She heard the question land. She didn’t change speed. “We did,” she said. “By posting, not by mouth.”\n\nThe push returned, sideways this time, younger. A trainee in a damp apron, glass dust still on her cuffs, blurted the question that had been living in more than one throat. “Why do we have to learn all this? Icons and slashes and cords and counts. Why us teaching us? Who decided you teach?” Her cheeks burned like someone who had worked hard and then been told it wasn’t real.\n\nJhace felt the question touch something he’d boxed and left on a mental shelf. He could map, set, pressline. He could feel the wrongness in a pin by standing near it. But who decided he should teach? He had said yes to work until work started saying yes back. Maybe that was all. Maybe it wasn’t enough.\n\n“Post first,” he told his hands, and made his hands obey. He slipped the second red slash onto the north wall and tugged the tie until it bit. Chloen chalked a clean rectangle on the ground where the cord would sit so the line had a visible shadow of authority. Kylar planted the cord hooks and stepped back, counting knot spacing with the same mouth‑steadying ritual he used for icon checks.\n\nTraffic pressed at the edges, unhappy and still obedient. Brighlee slid along the left flank, posting caller squares where reroute runners would stand. She flicked Blue to Yellow and back to Blue, letting the bell windows say the rest. The corridor started to re‑thread itself around the cordon’s new spine.\n\nTiffani tied a lower loop in the cord for small carts to be caught gently instead of the hard kind that tangles wheels. She set her shoulder to a post to test the give and nodded when the cord answered like a taught instrument. The trainee watched, breath fast.\n\nTiffani finally turned and let her voice carry as far as it needed, no farther. “Since the Bore,” she said, “the oceans did our cooling and our calming. We rested along the inner skin while the Great Settling took fifty years of storms and stupid wars out of people who thought they mattered more than gravity. When the dams rose, they quieted the water and handed us the bill. More bracing, more exactness, or the world starts cracking along our shortcuts.”\n\nShe set her palm to the cord and leaned into it until it held her weight and gave it back, like a span under a measured load. “Grindheim holds with metal,” she said, eyes on the trainee, on Red Sleeves, on Jhace without quite looking at him. “Strathmoor holds with glass. We read heat and we read weight. We teach each other because that’s how the halves keep being one piece.” She paused no beats longer than needed. “Don’t you know your history?” A dry breath later, sharper: “Learn your history.”\n\nJhace felt the words like a rivet going home. Not sermon, not spectacle. The truth sat in the room and didn’t ask permission. He discovered he’d been waiting for someone to say it out loud where the work lived.\n\nPressure didn’t care about the quality of a speech. A carrier stumbled at the cord’s edge. Jhace reached him first, a hand on the man’s forearm, a finger pointing to the reroute arrows on the Map‑as‑Set. “Right, then left,” he said, hoarse. The man nodded and complied. Crowd obeyed the card, not the man, which is the only obedience worth collecting.\n\nChloen finished the lane map overlay: a narrow chalk path on the stones that matched the arrow logic on the board. She stamped the small quill again, a reference, not a threat. “Cordon live,” she said. “Red slash posted. **L‑72 / P‑9** quarantined.”\n\n“Caller window to Four,” Brighlee said to the ringer down the way. Two fingers up, then a third to warn of a long note. She posted a smaller square three arm spans back from the cord so runners would stage instead of boiling the edge.\n\nRed Sleeves ground his jaw and then placed his palm flat on the ring glyph at waist height as he turned his cart to the reroute. He didn’t apologize. He didn’t need to. Behavior corrected public.\n\nThe trainee with the damp apron swallowed and looked at Tiffani like a person who had expected a slap and got a bench space instead. “We teach because we don’t get to be precious,” Tiffani added, tone flat as glass. “If we go clever, the corridor breaks. If the corridor breaks, the halves remember being halves the hard way.” She tightened a tie, just to keep her hands honest.\n\nKylar jogged the perimeter with a coil of ribbon to tag side stalls that needed their own small redirects. He touched the red slash on the placard each time he passed, building a personal ritual of what matters. Jhace watched him pick up the cadence without being programmed and felt the calf muscle that had been tight start to unclench at the edges. Work was learning him back.\n\n“Hoarse?” Brighlee asked, passing him a tin. He took a mouthful. The water tasted like clean iron. He nodded thanks. Cost comes due in little coins.\n\nA courier from the Couriers’ Bell skidded in, showed his card, and asked with his eyes. Jhace pointed him to the right lane with two fingers, then made the shape of the reroute with his hand. The boy answered with the same shape and vanished into the map as if the lane had always run that way. Competence wears no banner; it just stops bleeding.\n\nJall Var approached with a small audience and a mouth wired for exception. He scanned the placard, the cord, the chalk path, and the quill‑stamped overlays and found too few seams where a speech could wedge. “Duration,” he said, as if counting would save face.\n\n“Until **L‑72** is off every bench and its residues found or accounted,” Chloen said. “We’re mapping back to Annex shelves and forward to the last three racks that touched it. Charts posted. You can stand near them like a man.” She said it while writing, so the ink couldn’t hear tone and take it personally.\n\nJhace walked the length of the cord and checked ties. He pressed the ridger into the lower corner of the north placard a second time so a blind hand would know it was real. He felt the small heat at the base of his neck that told him fatigue would ask for a price later. He paid advance with a better knot.\n\nTiffani lifted the last of the red slashes from the bundle and handed it to him. Their fingers touched for half a second, nothing dramatized, only two metals exchanging a little heat. He tied the auxiliary placard on the mid‑wall by the soup carts so re‑entrants would be refused early. Then he looked back at the room and felt curiosity present but not exactly painful anymore. He didn’t know if he had earned teaching. He knew the board had earned obedience.\n\nKylar returned with a small tray containing two vials marked **L‑72** he’d pulled from a stall’s private shelf under color. He laid them on the shelf beside the bundle without speech. Salee would bless the boy if she saw the angle he had taken and the grace with which he had stepped out of it.\n\nChloen posted the lot map: a narrow sheet, icon‑only. Droplet glyph at top. Under it, **L‑72** and **P‑9** as plates, not letters, arranged in a ladder. Arrows running from Annex to stalls, to benches, to a pale circle around three carts that had touched product only by rumor. She pressed the quill square in the corner and added a second small ridged patch labeled **touch here** so fingers could read the card without eyes.\n\nJhace took the caller rod, not to tap cadence but to set the rod as a symbol on the caller post beside the new placard. He didn’t strike. The lane didn’t need the sound. It needed the shape.\n\n“Recall is live,” he said, not loud. The words didn’t matter as much as the sight: red slashes at both mouths, cord taut and respectful, map overlay chalk‑clean. The rerouted flow went right, then left, then through, like water finding the bed you show it.\n\nHe stepped back until his shoulders found the wall the way a man leans into a span to feel if it’s honest. The corridor answered at a lower pitch. Tires hissed, not screamed. Runners changed knees without looking down. A woman with jars in a sling touched the ring glyph without noticing and smiled because her weight finally belonged somewhere.\n\nChloen finished the notation at the bottom of the placard: **Quarantine: Lane B, today until cleared. Witness: Chloen; Setter: Jhace.** She dotted the colon with a steady hand and tied the last tie so it could be changed one‑handed in rain.\n\nAt the lane’s south mouth, the red‑slashed placard hung square in air that smelled like cooled metal. A thin rivulet ran down from the wall, met the card’s lower edge, and split around it to either side. Traffic saw the slash, read the lot, and turned without anyone shouting. The cord line held straight. The image was simple enough to teach a child or a steward. People moved the safer way."}
