{"name":"Novella 2 — Chapter 8: HALT — Narrowvale","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 8: HALT — Narrowvale\n\nTiffani reached the choke at the wrong kind of busy. The recall cord from **L‑72 / P‑9** still ran clean, red slashes bright at both mouths. Over it, cadence taps came ragged from a hand that didn’t own the rod. The pressline tried to speak while a Bridge crew pushed a barrow sideways against the map’s tooth. Someone in a veil apron waved heat for a corner drill that belonged in the Annex, not under a bell housing. Mixed crews. Mixed orders. The lane turned to noise.\n\nBrighlee stood at the caller post, blue square up for Four, one finger held in the air waiting for a note that would not organize anything. Kylar trotted the edge, correcting little knots that simply multiplied. Jall Var had a ledger under one arm and a voice aiming at the center of the world. “Press through the cord! Bridge first, then route to left!” he called. A junior from Weights Hall repeated it with the tone of a person who likes to hear himself in wet stone.\n\nTiffani felt the heat run wrong along the lane’s surface the way glass shows stress before it breaks. Two systems shouting their own names. She stepped to the posting wall and put her hand flat on the Map‑as‑Set, palm over the ring glyph. The board held its temperature, but the air around it felt like a span taught too fast. Her mouth tasted like the day had been a wire. Her fray band at her wrist, usually a quiet blue thread, went pale under damp.\n\n“Stop,” she tried first, to the rod hand, with the kind of voice that fits beside reactive material. He didn’t hear it. The taps arrived out of beat and got answered by panic rather than cadence. She saw a wheel slide on wet stone and a shoulder drop for a shove to make its case.\n\nShe picked a post instead of a person. “Brighlee,” she said. The courier was already unrolling the long fabric. Blue folded on blue in her hands like water behaving under orders, which is the rarity.\n\n“Jall Var,” Tiffani said. She kept her voice level. “Your order conflicts with a recall and a map. It crosses a pressline in surge. We are at risk.” He opened his mouth, ledger lifting like a shield. She was already moving.\n\nThe HALT lives in the kit behind a lock you must choose to open. She chose. The little key was cold against her palm. The box opened with the click of something that doesn’t argue with you later. Two banners the length of a cart and a half, blue as a good vein under skin. Two placards, icon‑only, the square with the quill and a ring bisected, the universal for still.\n\n“Kylar,” she said, and didn’t explain. He left his knot and took the lead corner of fabric like a runner tested on bells. Brighlee had the trailing edge and the ties already between her fingers. Tiffani took the center eyelet and felt the cloth’s weight load her shoulder like a bucket you swing correctly so it doesn’t hurt you.\n\nThey moved to the south mouth. The rod hand lifted for another set of bad taps. Tiffani didn’t look at him. She looked at the faces that wanted permission to be relieved. “Blue HALT,” she said. Not a command. A posting.\n\nBrighlee tied the first corner high on the caller post. Kylar looped the opposite into the wall hook that had no other use but this. Tiffani brought the center to the middle hook and pulled until the cloth cut the mouth horizontally at chest height. The banner hung straight. Rain struck, beaded, and stayed. The lane inhaled and did not exhale.\n\nJall Var tried volume. “You don’t have authority to shut a market!” His voice had a crack in it that weather wasn’t responsible for.\n\nTiffani stepped in under the banner’s center and set the first blue placard on the wall with two ties and a ridged bite across its legend strip. She stamped the quill square and then a second blue seal space beneath it left blank for the clear that would come later. This was the post, not the cure. She printed the icon for still—ring bisected—large. Under it she set three small plates: droplet, wedge, arrows all crossed with a thin blue bar. The bar said no Bridge, no Pressline, no Veil. She pressed the ridger again so fingers could know.\n\nNorth mouth. Same motions, faster, because once you set one end the crowd has to see the second or it invents a corridor in its own head and tries to use it. Kylar was there already because a runner’s job is to arrive where the next action belongs. Brighlee had a spare tie in her teeth, a habit she hated and permitted only under rain.\n\nThey hung the second banner. Blue took the light differently than red; it made the wet look like order rather than hazard. Tiffani tied knots loose enough for later hands and tight enough for now. Her thumb stung where a dry crack had split under lamp heat two chapters ago; she ignored it and let blood be a private story.\n\n“By what law,” Jall Var demanded, because he had nothing else left, “do you hold commerce?”\n\nTiffani didn’t spare him word. She slid the Conclave reference out of the placard packet and pressed the quill into the corner where people who like boxes can find the box they like. “By posted,” she said to the board, not the man. She lifted the ridger and pressed a long, firm bite into the lower edge so it would tell on any hand trying to remove it without being seen.\n\nThe pressline rod dropped its beat and then stopped. The hand holding it looked at the blue cloth as if it were a river’s edge. Bridge crews stilled without knowing whether they had decided to. A man trying to be brave with a full cart got brave correctly and set his brake. The soup stall turned down its flame until steam fell to a curl, then a thread.\n\nTiffani walked the center of the corridor. The cord for recall remained, red slash living under blue like a vein under a bruise. She checked its tension and found it honest. She passed Valaryn at the edge, who had left the Annex only far enough to read a corridor pulse and had already set a **Quiet Room** slip on the clinic board at the end of the lane, a little bell inked beside it. The cost now, not later; a space for frayed hands to sit out of the weather and not make weather of their own.\n\nHer own fray band had gone pale, almost white against her wrist. She turned it once with a finger and let the gesture fix nothing but mark that she’d noticed. The cost would come due as tightness at the base of her neck and a hand that shook when it had no right to. For now, she gave the lane all the steadiness she’d saved in muscle.\n\nCrowd tried to do what crowds do in a vacuum and told jokes that weren’t funny. Tiffani gave them a job instead. “Tools to hooks,” she said to the mixed crews. Work lays down better when it knows its place. Veil rollers went on their pegs. Pressline rod went on the caller hook with a small fabric tie around it to keep it from thinking it might fall and start something. Bridge bars stacked clean at the wall.\n\nBrighlee moved along the blue line and posted a smaller notice square at each side alley: **HALT live. Wait. Caller posts idle.** Icon only, quill small. Kylar took a lap without running and made sure no one tried clever, especially where clever meant rerouting through shops and making enemies that would last all week.\n\nJall Var tried a new tack, low and poisoned. “Do you know the losses you write with your banners, veld?” He used the caste name like it owed him.\n\nShe let the rain answer that one. He would get his ledger sorted soon enough when the clear posted with a seal he could read like a coin. She made a mental line at the bottom of the page: **Today’s cost is less than a broken span and a panicked lane.** She didn’t write it. She posted it in what she did next.\n\nShe returned to the south mouth and completed the artifact. Under the blue placard’s icon field she set narrow plates for role: **setter** blank, **witness** blank, **time** blank. She inked the time. She left setter and witness for what would happen after the fix. She pressed a small crosshatch patch in the corner, the place for strangers to put a thumb and feel real. She felt it herself to take the edge off the tremor that wanted to be seen.\n\nThe air in Narrowvale changed pitch. No wheels. No taps. The only sound was rain flattening itself on stone and the wet mutter of people forced to breathe instead of perform. A child reached for the ring glyph out of habit and hesitated, hand hovering an inch shy of contact like a bird learning the shape of a window. His mother caught the wrist and moved it to her own palm, an acceptable substitute.\n\nBrighlee found her shoulder. “Blue posted end to end,” she said, quiet. Kylar stood at the north banner with the posture of a hinge: ready, not moving. Chloen arrived, maps under oilcloth, eyes sharp and already empty of panic. “Combined fix,” Chloen said, a plan more than words. Pressline rhythm to match reroute, Bridge angle corrected to the new tooth, Veil windows done as windows rather than leaks. There would be witnesses, signatures, an honest clear.\n\nTiffani nodded once. She felt the Quiet Room bell in her head even before it rang on the clinic board, a clear tone that hangs without nagging. She let her shoulders drop one grain. The lane held its breath and did not use the breath for anything stupid.\n\nShe checked the north banner’s ties one last time. She took the pressline rod off its hook and laid it in the hooks properly, shaft cradle set, so the next hand wouldn’t reach wrong. She tightened the middle knot on the Bridge bar stack so they wouldn’t roll and look like a cure.\n\nThe steward stood under a canopy and calculated how to attach himself to the clear when it came. Good. Let him rehearse the right story. The mixed crews sorted themselves by tool habit and waited, heat leaving their hands into metal and wood where it could be useful later.\n\nThe blue cloths moved a little in the damp wind and then stilled. Drops gathered along their lower edge like mercury thinking about a change and deciding against it. The placards’ ridged patches gathered a small collection of thumbprints, each one a stranger teaching their own nerves to respect a posted line.\n\nTiffani lifted the last placard from the kit, the small one that lives in the middle of the corridor at eye height to tell approaching bodies what the big banners already say. She tied it with two‑finger knots. She pressed the quill. She pressed the ridge. She stepped back.\n\nBlue HALT banners hung across both mouths, cloth heavy with damp. Placards below them read still in icon language anyone could learn without a clerk. Along the wall, tools sat on hooks like well‑behaved thoughts. The street held its breath and did not spend it."}

{"name":"Novella 2 — Chapter 9: Witnessed Clear","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 9: Witnessed Clear\n\nJhace arrived to the blue cloths holding. Banners cut the mouths clean, rain rounding their lower edge into beads that refused to fall until they wanted. Under them the cord for **L‑72 / P‑9** still ran, slack taken right. People on both sides wore the look of a place waiting for its own lungs. The posting wall held the HALT placard Tiffani had set, the witness lines blank. You don’t clear a blue by talking near it. You clear it by making the lane safe in public.\n\n“Combined fix,” he said to the nearest crews, ribs tight from a day of taps and corrections. “Pressline pairs only from caller. Bridge angle to the new tooth. Veil windows by count, not courage.” He pointed to each tool in turn so the bodies attached to them remembered what they were for.\n\nChloen unfurled a chalk map she’d kept dry under oilcloth. She ghosted the reroute on the stones with a square heel, matching arrow logic to what the corridor had already been taught. Brighlee stood at the caller post, blue square ready to flip when the cadence could be trusted. Kylar brought the short hammer and the ties, shoulder sheen showing he’d run the perimeter twice without being asked.\n\nTiffani crossed to him from the north banner, veil roller at her side, hood back now that the air had settled. Her eyes took the room in a pane at a time. “Windows at two and six,” she said, a glassworker’s weather sense laid on a street. He nodded and felt the small, ridiculous spark in his chest at the way her hands measured air. Competence has a heat of its own. He boxed the thought and put it where it wouldn’t spill into the work.\n\nJall Var hovered with a ledger’s sour patience. Onlookers collected like filings near a magnet: vendors, a ringer off his rope, two Weights clerks guarding their faces. Pressure lived in schedules and the wet cold that found wrists. Jhace could feel his own voice frayed from the hours. He saved it by pointing.\n\nHe took the rod and set his palm to the caller post to read the wood. The lane’s tone had climbed to a string about to ping. He lifted the rod and gave the pairs soft and exact.\n\nTap tap.\n\nThe sound reached just far enough. Brighlee flipped Blue to Yellow and tied it. Kylar touched the ring glyph at waist height and made a small circle with one finger, telling the front of the knot to inhale and not step. The knot listened because the taps remembered how to be true. Two short. Then nothing. Two short again.\n\nThe Bridge crew took the bar at the choke’s tooth. Jhace set his boot against the stone and tipped the bar three degrees to seat into the corrected bite that had come out of the Yard that morning. “Feather,” he said, and they eased pressure until the map’s tooth accepted their angle and the wheel that had been eager to jump took the lane instead. The bar moved; the lane moved with it.\n\nTiffani posted herself at the first window and laid the veil with a count the Annex had taught into her bones. One to find heat, two to move it, three to leave. She didn’t touch the gloss; she coaxed it into matte like a person persuading a river to remember its bed. Steam curled once and forgot to be dramatic. She slid to the second window. In profile, her focus was all edges and economy. The pane held. Jhace felt the heat he’d shelved return at the base of his throat, unwanted and not unwelcome. He kept his hands in the work.\n\nChloen chalked a crescent at the tight corner and mirrored it on the board with a small overlay tile so heads could track what feet were doing. Kylar re‑tied the caller’s midline knot so the post would not creep under use. A soup seller, sensing the temperature of the place, turned his flame low until the steam behaved.\n\n“Pairs,” Jhace said to himself and the post. Tap tap. Silence. Tap tap. He kept the spacing with his ribs, not his watch. The crowd’s hum lowered by a note. Red Sleeves, if it was him or his cousin again, put his palm to the ring at reflex and then issued himself to the reroute without mouth.\n\nAt the second window Tiffani lifted the veil and the air under it settled. She spoke without looking up. “Next window after the bridge bite seats,” she said. He answered with a nod she might not have seen and liked the chance that she had. His wrist ached in the old way; his calves held from the day of standing on rain‑polished stone. He let the ache be part of the price of a clear anyone could use.\n\nValaryn appeared like a quiet stitch at the edge, took one look at the lane and posted a new **Quiet Room** slip with the little bell drawn darker. One porter went without argument, shoulders lower on exit than on entrance. The lane liked that.\n\nBridge bite seated. He could feel it in the bar the crew held and in the tone of the stones under his boot. He set the pairs twice more to teach cadence to the part of the crowd that would be late to the lesson.\n\nTap tap. Tap tap.\n\n“Window,” Tiffani said. She laid it. Jhace watched the gloss surrender to matte and let himself notice the fine fog standing off the veil edge and the bone‑honest way her wrist didn’t tremble even now. Heat climbed his neck. He clipped it back into its place with the thought he had used since he was a boy at the Yard: work first, want later if there’s a later.\n\nChloen lifted her hand and drew a straight line from chalk to board, then stamped the quill square on the overlay. “Route holds,” she said, flat and satisfied. Brighlee flicked two fingers to the ringer. The bell gave the clean note of a window that would be kept. People heard it and relaxed without deciding to.\n\n“Setter present,” Tiffani said, moving to the south mouth. She put her palm on the HALT placard’s blank line, not yet writing, just making a shape with her hand that told the object what was coming. “Witness ready?”\n\nJhace gave the pairs one last time and then hung the rod on its hook. He felt his posture slip an inch toward tired and let it. Drained is honest. He walked to the placard where she waited.\n\n“Ready,” he said.\n\nShe held up the blue seal, heavy as a small coin, inked with darker blue. The CLEAR lives under HALT like the second face of a tool. You don’t stamp it until the lane shows you it deserves it.\n\n“North check,” she said. They went together. Kylar ran ahead to touch ties and hooks. Bridge bars sat in their cradle like well‑behaved thought. The cord still held the quarantine under the blue; not everything was ending, only the unsafe overlap. The reroute flowed like a taught river. He pointed left, then right. Runners matched the shapes. No panic lived in any of it.\n\n“South check,” she said. They returned. Brighlee stood with the ledger, water running off the oilcloth brim onto her boot. Chloen held the overlay tile in two fingers, ready to replace it with the map’s old face if that was the better story of the next hour. Jall Var had found a way to look like he had waited all day to approve this.\n\nTiffani looked at the lane as if it were a pane under lamp. Her breath evened in a way he recognized from benches, not streets. “Witness,” she said again, now to him. He had been as much cause of the knot as anyone with a rod or a bar. He could be cause of the steady too.\n\nHe nodded. “Witness.” The word fit his mouth.\n\nShe set the coin to the placard’s lower field. He put his palm over hers, not touching skin, just giving the backing a pressure it needed to take the bite. Heat jumped across the small air between them, the old kind that once would have made him step away and now just made him focused. She pressed. The wood changed tone under both their hands. When she lifted the seal, the blue circle lay flat and darker. No ridges to catch weather. No curl for a thief to lift. The ink sat like a statement.\n\nBrighlee recorded the time. Chloen wrote **Combined correction: Pressline cadence restored; Bridge bite seated; Veil windows held.** Kylar, grinning without showing teeth, loosed the first banner tie. The cloth gave up its tension slowly, folding into itself with the relief of muscle that has been allowed to stop.\n\nJhace walked to the north mouth and furled the second banner. He gathered the wet weight into his arms and felt it pull at his elbows the way a tired child does near the end of a market day. He set the roll into the kit and locked it. One HALT per day, one per need, not a habit.\n\nHe returned to the placard. Tiffani inked the lines. **Setter: Tiffani veld. Witness: Jhace brast. Clear time:** she wrote it. She pressed the ridger across the legend for a second bite so strangers could prove it later without asking anyone. She read the board with her thumb the way he had seen her read glass.\n\nThe lane exhaled. Wheels took purchase like they remembered how. The ring glyph at waist height shone where palms touched it without noticing. Two floor hands who had looked ready to fight five minutes ago were arguing quietly about a recipe instead. Steam rose from the soup stall in a line that didn’t throw tantrums.\n\n“Blue furl confirmed,” Brighlee said from the far mouth. “Caller post live.” She flipped Yellow to Blue for the next weather. The bell gave two clean notes to bless the seam.\n\nJhace’s limbs informed him that they were heavier than ideals. He rolled his right wrist and felt the tendon complain and settle. He allowed himself to look at Tiffani once as if she were part of the posting. The wet at her hairline, the steady mouth, the way her shoulders had dropped one grain when the coin bit. Want ran along his skin like a small current under a rail, inconvenient and very much alive. He let it be a warm truth he would fold later with care if later was given.\n\nShe didn’t turn, only spoke toward the board as if to the city. “We clear what we set,” she said. Then, a fraction lower and for him alone, “Good work, brast.” The word lit every place that had been tired and made it feel earned instead of spent. He didn’t answer. He didn’t need to.\n\nHe stepped back into the weather to see the full frame, because that is how he closes anything worth closing. The banners were rolls under arms now, not declarations. The HALT placard bore the blue coin of the seal, flattened into a dark circle that lay like weight where a gust couldn’t argue it up. Above it, the map and cadence read as if they had been written on stone from the start. The crowd knew what to do without shouting.\n\nKylar wiped water from the lower edge of the placard and then, because he is a boy who learns by finger, pressed his thumb to the smooth blue seal. It did not push back. It didn’t need to.\n\nOn the posted board, the blue seal lay flat as a dark coin. Along the lane, the furled banners hung from Brighlee’s arm like a coil of calm rope."}

{"name":"Novella 2 — Chapter 10: Micro‑Bind at the Clinic Door","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 10: Micro‑Bind at the Clinic Door\n\nTiffani reached the Annex arch at shift change and felt the pressure before she saw it. Bodies gathered in a crescent, shoulders turned toward the cool promise inside. The big timber door complained along its lower hinge, a long dry note that made people push to end it. Pushing made it worse. The sound ran in her jaw like a file.\n\n“Hold,” she said, voice kept low to fit the material. Valaryn was already there with return‑to‑work slips clipped at elbow height, redirecting the impatient by paper instead of mouth. Kylar had posted the small **caller** square on the outer lintel and was tracing the ring glyph at waist height with a thumb, setting the habit for the next person.\n\nA second surge touched the arch and tried to become a practice. The timber answered with a deeper creak that promised a jam. Tiffani stepped to the hinge and put her palm flat to feel heat. Wood was dry but the iron pin was warmer than it should be; friction had been earning a wage all morning.\n\n“Micro‑Bind,” she said, and the people who had been trained made space for the word. She set the brace hooks, two short lengths under the bench like spare bones. “We act as one. One hand on home line. Caller counts.” She tipped her chin at Kylar. “Two short.”\n\nTap tap, his little hammer said against the post. The sound traveled a handspan past the first row of shoulders and then returned better.\n\nShe slid the first brace into the notch she’d chalked last week and felt it take the door’s weight. “Left foot to stone seam. Ring to waist,” she said to the two volunteers closest. A porter with a wrapped wrist and an older woman with kettle scars set their palms to the ring glyph she had chalked on the timber months ago for exactly this. Hands on home; hands not free to improvise.\n\nThe hinge pulled wrong again. She felt it bite and then let it go. “On two,” she said. “Bind.” Kylar gave the pairs. Tap tap. They leaned as one, not like a shove but like a person standing up from a bench together with old friends. The door moved on its pin without protest for half its arc, then gave another long complaint as the weight shifted to the lower hinge again.\n\nSalee arrived breathless from the inner hall with a vial of thin oil and the ledger for door maintenance. “Dry,” she said, adding nothing unhelpful. Tiffani took the vial without looking and cooled her breath across the spout so the first dribble wouldn’t run. She touched the pin’s shoulder with one measured drop, then a second. The door took the oil like a tired hand takes warm water.\n\nShe could have called Jhace, but he was at the north mouth closing out the clear and checking ties. The thought of him arrived uninvited: how his shoulder sets when he takes a brace alone, the way he doesn’t waste a joint. Heat rose under her collarbone as if a lamp had been turned one wick up. She moved the thought out of the way the same way she moved steam off a corner: one, two, three, and let it settle to matte.\n\n“Again,” she said. “On two.” Tap tap. They leaned; the braces held; the door followed. The creak shortened by a finger’s worth. Progress.\n\nThe crowd at the back tried to snake in on the second beat, as crowds do when a system shows a seam. Valaryn’s hand lifted—not a scold, a gate. She posted a small **wait** square with the clinic ⭕ and a tiny bell drawn beside it. The square looked like a favor instead of a refusal. People breathed. The pressure redistributed from spike to slope.\n\nTiffani knelt to sight along the lower edge where timber met stone. The sill had a proud point that had learned to raise itself under years of bad weather and yesterday’s surge. She tapped the stone twice with the handle to print its memory in her wrist and decided it could be asked to behave without cutting. “Left brace a thumb higher,” she said. “Right brace, feather. Don’t drag.”\n\nThey moved, one unit. The porter’s wrapped hand shook and then steadied when his palm found the ring again. The older woman hummed under her breath—only a note, no tune. Kylar called the pairs so soft the sound lived more in the wood than in the air.\n\nThe door passed through the last third of its arc. The creak dropped from a raw line to a shallow scrape. Salee touched the pin with the remaining oil, then wiped the excess so grit wouldn’t turn gift to grit later. “Ledger,” she said to herself, making a note by habit. Tiffani’s jaw unclenched half a grain.\n\nInside, a trainee tried to help by pulling from the far side. Helping is often sabotage with good manners. “Stop,” Tiffani said, a palm raised where the trainee could see it through the hinge gap. “Outside binds, inside waits.” The girl let go and stitched her hands into her own belt to keep them from inventing ideas.\n\nThey set the door to half‑open and held it there for a count. The timber let heat go. The iron cooled a degree. Tiffani touched pin and plate again, not trusting wishes. Honest metal answered with a duller feel, the good dull like rain settling into a pan.\n\n“Again,” she said. Tap tap. They took the door through the full motion. This time the timber offered a a long thrummed sigh that resolved into silence at the seat. The sound left her jaw at last; she felt the ache she’d been holding like a nail loosen.\n\nJhace arrived on the edge of her vision at the corner of the arch, water running off his coat’s hem onto the stone where it belonged. He did what he does best: assessed and then didn’t interfere. He kept one hand on the home ring by the jamb and offered his other to the brace without talking. Their eyes met for the length of a single beat. The spark that had been shelved put a warm bead in her throat and slid down to somewhere that made balance both harder and easier. She didn’t smile. She didn’t need to. Work first. Always.\n\n“Card,” Tiffani said. Salee was already flipping open the board kit. The current door drill card hung on the inner wall: wedge glyph small, ring glyph at waist height, three bars to mark count, a droplet for oil icon, and the clinic ⭕ in the corner. It lacked the bind clause that experience had just taught into their bones.\n\nTiffani pulled a blank from the kit and set it on the bench. She inked the wedge larger and moved it upstream of the ring to teach “set corner first” even at a door’s hinge. Under it she printed a simple bind icon: a bar crossing a ring with two small hands shown as half‑circles, not pretty, true. **Micro‑Bind: one unit, caller pairs** went in icon plates, not text. She stamped the clinic ⭕ in the lower right and the small Conclave quill square bottom left for standard reference.\n\nShe pressed the ridger into the legend strip so a wet thumb could test the card without looking. Then she added a second patch at the lower edge—a crosshatch—so that strangers would have a sanctioned place to touch and learn. “Kylar,” she said, and he was at her shoulder with ties in order: top, side, side, bottom.\n\nThey posted the amended card where hands could find it without scraping knuckles. Ties loose enough to adjust, tight enough to defy weather. She rubbed the ridged patch with her thumb and felt her own nerves settle in response to the honesty of texture.\n\n“Run the drill,” she said. Valaryn moved to the outer edge to count bodies and call who entered on which beat. Kylar tapped pairs at the post. The porter and the kettle‑scar woman took their places without being told. The door performed like a simple machine that had been respected from the start. No creak. Only the mild complaint of well‑behaved wood, which is how wood talks when you listen first.\n\nShe let a breath out that she hadn’t admitted she’d been holding. The cost showed as a pressure along her molars that had nowhere to go now. It climbed to her temples and then faded like heat leaving a pane at count three. She sat on the cool step as the next group passed in, letting the chill reset her jaw. Jhace’s boots came into her peripheral—a scuff from stone, not a swagger. He stayed at the ring a moment longer than the work strictly required. The warmth returned, a small, ridiculous ribbon under her sternum. She kept it, folded twice.\n\nSalee posted a small addendum square under the new card: **oil check each bell** with a droplet and a little bell glyph. She initialed it with her tidy hand. The room liked that; the line moved without a voice raising. A baby slept in a sling two bodies back; its breath matched the pairs.\n\nTiffani stood and made one last check. She reached to the hinge and laid her finger against the pin’s shoulder. The metal felt like calm weather. She set her palm flat to the timber and asked if it had learned anything. The door answered by holding its own weight without leaning on anyone’s temper.\n\nShe lifted the ridger and pressed a second bite into the amended card’s lower edge so anyone could prove the card real without having to ask a clerk. She flipped the old card, wrote **superseded** in tiny plates with the quill square, and slid it into the kit.\n\nTraffic took the doorway in steady fours. Valaryn moved the **wait** square one body at a time to make the queue honest without anyone’s pride paying for it. Kylar tapped the pairs as lightly as he could and still be heard. Salee’s ledger closed with a clean thap.\n\nTiffani let her shoulder touch stone again for one heartbeat, then another. Her jaw released. The door sat easy in its frame. On the inner wall, the amended drill card hung at eye height, icons clean, ridges proud. Outside, the long creak that had argued for an hour lost its case and went quiet."}

{"name":"Novella 2 — Chapter 11: Weights Purge","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 11: Weights Purge\n\nJhace came into Weights Hall with the stamp kit under his coat and rain still draining down his sleeves. The hall always sounded like money trying not to admit it was metal: coin on stone, scale pans kissing, a clerk’s quill ticking like a slow leak. People crowded shoulder‑to‑shoulder along the benches, each of them certain the day owed them a weight that liked their side of the balance better.\n\nMobel had made his stage plain and public. A burn pan sat on a tripod in the center, charcoal already woken to a clean low heat. Tongs, ladle, broom. Beside it, the new serial stones board lay blank on the posting easel, ties ready. A row of true weights waited under a cloth, each one stamped with a sequence that could be followed forward or back like a good story. On the table, suspect stones: too glossy, edges too proud, chips that told of soft guts.\n\n“Clerk,” Mobel said without looking up, voice sanded flat by years of numbers. “Start with the half‑stones. That’s where the cheats live.” He wore his Mercers’ badge like an iron coin above his breastbone. His mouth was a straight line that had learned not to apologize for procedure in front of anger.\n\nJhace nodded and set the stamp kit down near the board. Brighlee took her place with a small ledger for serials and a separate page for burns. Kylar hovered on the edge of the crowd with a coil of cord in case the line needed a polite spine. Tiffani leaned in shadow by the door column, veil work done for the hour, watching the crowd the way a glassworker watches a cooling pane for stress. She caught his eye and tipped the smallest degree of chin. The heat that had been shelved since the clear put a neat ember under his ribs. He set it aside the way you set a tool you want but don’t need yet.\n\nMobel lifted the cloth. “A stone is not a promise,” he said to the room. “It is an object with a number. If the number doesn’t live in sequence, the stone is ash.” He set a true half‑stone on the scale and let the pan kiss the plate. The needle came home without wobble. He flipped it, showing the serial: **H‑3417**. Brighlee read it back, hand already writing. Mobel set it on the “known” row.\n\nA vendor with sleeves too clean volunteered his half‑stone with theatrical generosity. Mobel took it without thanks and rolled it in his palm to feel the bite around the edge. The edge was wrong: the chamfer a hair shallow, the stamp too deep, the serial trying to persuade rather than identify. **H‑3491**. Mobel checked the sequence. There was no 3491 between 3479 and 3480 because the Conclave had reset the die there after a flood. He did not announce that part loudly. He spoke to the object.\n\n“Stone out of family,” Mobel said. People at three benches leaned as if they could read numbers from his skin. He set the suspect on the pan with a true at the other side. The needle drifted to true then quivered as if the stone couldn’t decide what lie it wanted to be. He lifted the suspect, weighed it against three more knowns, and watched the quiver repeat.\n\n“We burn,” he said.\n\nThe sleeves‑clean vendor’s mouth prepared to argue lineage and inheritance. Mobel ignored the mouth and addressed the board. “Serial stones board lives today.” He handed the placard to Jhace.\n\nAction before sermon. Jhace inked the board in icon plates: a row of small squares for stone icons, a ladder of numbers, a quill‑square for Conclave reference in the top right, Mercers’ Table stamp in the left. Under the icons he printed the plate series for halves and whole stones: **H** and **W** plates, not letters, so anyone could track with a finger. He set three columns: **issued**, **retired**, **burned**. He pressed the ridger into the legend strip and then again in a small crosshatch patch in the lower corner for testing.\n\nMobel poised the suspect above the pan with the tongs. “This is not a punishment,” he said. “This is a reset.” He lowered the stone until heat claimed it. The glossy lie dulled first, then turned matte in a skin. A crack whispered around the edge like a rumor finding a wall. The stone split with a sound that pleased no one. The crowd breathed through their teeth anyway because the noise told the truth in a dialect everyone understood.\n\nTiffani made a tiny sound at the column, approval not for pain but for the way heat makes wrong shapes admit their nature. Jhace caught himself wanting to stand closer just to share that acknowledgment and then let the wanting fall through the floorboards and lie there until later. He lifted the stamp and set the Mercers’ seal dark in the board’s top left corner.\n\nBrighlee read out serials as Mobel moved. “H‑3417 issued; H‑3420 issued; H‑3479 issued; H‑3480 issued; H‑3491 burned.” She wrote and then drew a small red dot beside the burned line that matched the heat of the pan in miniature. Kylar moved through the line, collecting two more halves from men whose faces said they were not sure what their faces should say.\n\nJall Var had arrived with the smell of paper dried near a hearth. He found a place to stand where an audience could see him see and practiced his neutrality in case it might be mistaken for leadership. Weights clerks from the Hall stood behind Mobel, hands folded the way people fold when they trust their table more than a crowd.\n\nThe second suspect, **H‑3429**, sat good in the hand but bad in sequence. Mobel didn’t announce; he tested. The pan sang wrong. He burned it. The crack landed like a small hammer on people’s foreheads and knocked loose arguments they had stored there. A woman two rows back reached for the ring glyph printed on the board’s lower field and rubbed it with the same absent habit she’d learned at the choke. The ring took her nerve and gave it back calmer.\n\nJhace kept the board honest. He inked the numbers, pressed ridges, stamped. His thumb picked up black until the pad looked like it belonged to a charcoal burner. He wiped it once on his coat and accepted the stain. Attention wants to stray when you have repeated work; he taxed it with a small ritual: index on ridged patch, eyes on quill square, breath in for four and out for six. The beat steadied his hand. Cost came as a scuff in focus he would pay back with a quiet minute later and a chew of clinic salts.\n\nMobel moved to whole stones. “Counterfeits are lazier here,” he said. “They rely on fear of coin to outrun inspection.” He took a heavy **W‑221** from a man with honest callus and a worried mouth. “If it’s true, you get to keep it. If it’s not, you get to keep your honesty instead.” He set it on the pan and gave it a good chance to behave. It did. The needle settled like a bird finding a branch. He showed the serial; Brighlee wrote **issued** and smiled without moving her lips.\n\nA boy at the edge called out before he could stop himself. “Why not just trust the scale?” He was cut off by an elbow from his mother. Mobel answered anyway, not losing his place. “Because a liar brings his own pan.” The room let the line sit. He lifted the next suspect with tongs and found the soft edge that tells a file did the finishing instead of a die. He burned it. The crack this time popped louder and drew a flinch from men who thought they were too old to flinch.\n\nTiffani had moved closer, not to be seen but to read faces. Her shoulder brushed his briefly when she leaned to see which quill plate he was using. The small contact ran a wire up his arm and made the hair at his wrist notice air. He kept the stamp straight. The wire stayed. He took one breath too deep and pretended it was because of the room’s heat.\n\nMobel finished the halves. The suspect pile had dwindled to two stones that pretended they wanted to be judged last. He refused the drama. He set the first on the pan and then lifted it and held it up for the room to see. “Serial isn’t a crowning,” he said. “It’s a map.” He turned the stone to show the bite pattern around the numbers. The teeth had left the wrong scallop—too even, no bite hills. “A smooth story means someone shaved the edges.” He burned that one slower to show the skin change. The crack made a tidy seam.\n\nBrighlee posted the running board beside him. Kylar tightened the ties so the board sat flush and could be read without anyone needing to touch it. Jhace pressed the final stamp into the lower margin: **Mobel / Mercers’ Table** in plate, no text. He added a second ridged patch on the lower edge so the blind habit could learn here, too.\n\nThe last suspect was a pride thing. The man who had brought it forward really wanted it to hold. Mobel had him set it on the pan himself. The needle paused at true as if it didn’t want a fight, then walked as the stone cooled in air and showed its soft heart. “Ash,” Mobel said, not unkind. The man lifted his chin and watched the burn. He didn’t ask for exceptions. The crack put the room into its quietest quiet.\n\nJhace stepped back to see the frame the way he has to when he can feel his focus narrow to a dangerous point. The board read like a ladder: issued clean in a left column, retired with a soft mark in the center, burned with red dots and a tiny flame plate for today only, a teaching embellishment Mobel allowed when the room needed to feel the lesson. The Mercers’ seal sat dark. The quill square sat opposite. The ring glyph at waist height shone where people had steadied themselves without asking truth to be gentle.\n\nJall Var cleared his throat to be associated with closure. Mobel pretended the sound belonged to the room and not a person. He lifted the pan with tongs and tipped the cracked halves into a waiting tray. They looked like river stones someone had broken without malice. He set the tray where the line could look and decide not to buy from men who made such things.\n\n“Serials posted,” Brighlee said. Her voice was a nail with just enough bite to hold the board to the wall forever or until the next audit. “Burns recorded.” She wiped ink from her thumb with a cloth that had stopped pretending to be white years ago.\n\nJhace felt the black on his own thumb and the fine grit under the nail from the ridger’s teeth. He rolled his wrist and the tendon reminded him he had tapped a lane to life more than once this week. He glanced sideways. Tiffani was watching the board, but her mouth had the almost smile she gets when a process lands in a crowd without needing a sermon. She felt him looking or maybe felt the heat that looking made. Her eyes slid and met his for the length of a breath. The wire tightened and then softened, tension turning into the kind of anticipation that doesn’t ask to be fed right now, only promised a later.\n\nHe put his thumb to the board’s ridged patch and let his body settle to the texture’s small insistence. Then he stepped forward and tied the lower right corner so the board didn’t develop a curl in damp.\n\nMobel raised his chin to the room. “This corridor will not lose coin to soft stone,” he said. “If you carry a weight without a place in the sequence, bring it to the pan or don’t bring it at all.” He set the tongs on their hook. The crowd murmured quiet instead of noise. Men who came to be angry found themselves nodding because the board had collected their argument and set it on fire in front of them.\n\nJhace took one last look for contradictions. None. He closed the stamp kit and wiped his thumb again without success. The black would live there until morning. Fine.\n\nOn the posting wall, the serial stones board hung square: **H** and **W** ladders inked, Mercers’ seal biting the corner, quill square across from it, two ridged patches for fingers to learn the truth without reading. In the burn pan, the last cracked fake stone cooled to a dull gray, split clean down its lie while the crowd’s murmur settled to something like respect."}

{"name":"Novella 2 — Chapter 12: Map‑as‑Set Expansion","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 12: Map‑as‑Set Expansion\n\nTiffani reached Narrowvale’s west mouth when the lamps were already tired. Night crews rushed a kind of hurry that thinks speed is a skill. Steam had gone thin; the kettle men packed in half‑steps, hitting each other with handles and apologies. Three chokes in play: West Mouth, Center Notch, East Gate. Three boards to standardize before the bell turned and night invented its own rules.\n\n“Boards first,” she said, low, to the bodies who could hear and be changed by it. Brighlee held the ties and the caller squares; Kylar had the ridger wrapped and dry; Chloen brought the plates in a small crate like a traveling shop. Jhace was a darker shape near the Center Notch, bar in hand, making a bar look like patience. She let the sight put a small heat where it could live without wasting her hands.\n\nJall Var arrived with a parchment habit and three clerks behind him, each with a mouth ready to engrave exceptions. “Night crews require posted text,” he said. “No one reads icons at this hour.” He enjoyed being wrong in complicated sentences.\n\n“People read,” Tiffani said, which meant fingers and bodies. She stepped to the bare board at the West Mouth and chalked the rectangle with two practiced breaths. “We’ll keep pace for them.” She set the kit on the wall shelf and opened it. Plates sat in order: arrows, wedge, ring, bell codes, droplet. Quill square in felt. She dipped her thumb in ink, tested drag, and let the wet tell her how much bite the ridger would need to hold under damp.\n\nAction first: she placed the arrows. Three lanes in one frame. Left: heavy carts. Center: foot and hand‑barrows. Right: return. She set the tails in a small tooth to declare the choke without theater. Pull clean. Ink sat proud; rain thought about it and decided not to interfere. She set the ring glyph at waist height where palms would find it without asking. She measured from her own belt and corrected for the shorter reach of Kylar’s hand because the board belonged to strangers, not to her.\n\nWedge glyph next, upstream of the corner that had taught too many people to believe brinkmanship was a plan. Large and honest, angle true. Then the bell marks: two short, a gap, two short, because cadence is how a lane tells tired minds which piece of time is theirs.\n\nChloen held the overlay tile for this choke against the board’s lower edge, checking that the map on wood matched the one in chalk underfoot. “West matches,” she said. “Center Notch after.”\n\nJall Var inhaled to recite a clause. Brighlee put a caller square in his visual path, Blue on the outer face, and the steward’s mouth adjusted to accommodate something that wasn’t him. He didn’t sign; he didn’t have to. The Conclave square would do the talking he wanted credit for later.\n\nTiffani pressed the quill square into the top right. The wood changed tone. She pressed the ridger into the legend strip halfway down so fingers could verify. Kylar slid the board’s ties through and she set them loose‑tight so they could be shifted one‑handed. Cost started behind her eyes, a pressure that made light smear if she blinked poorly. She didn’t blink poorly. She finished the West in under a minute and took the kit under her arm.\n\nCenter Notch was the loudest. Night crews trying to make up time by pretending to be the day. Jhace had placed the bar low, at the ankle of the choke, where it teaches rather than argues. He saw her arrival and stepped back a half pace with a tilt of wrist that said, this is your surface. Heat moved under her skin, a neat stripe from throat to sternum. She kept both hands on tools.\n\nShe chalked the rectangle. Arrows first. The tails met at the tooth, which she set one knuckle south of where it had been in the afternoon because the cobble there carried a slick she could feel through her boot. She laid the ring glyph, then the wedge. She placed the bell marks and caught the faint blur in her right eye at the edge of her reach. Night and small marks. Eye strain pulled like wire.\n\n“Light,” she said, and Kylar was there with a hand lamp raised to shoulder height, elbow tucked to minimize shake. The light behaved. She bit the legend with the ridger and let the teeth sing against the ink. Raised, proud, unambiguous. Chloen set the overlay tile to match. “Center matches,” Chloen said. “East will need a mirror.”\n\nA teamster tried to squeeze a cart through the center under her arm while the ink still wet. Jhace moved without the slop of urgency, one palm to ring height, one finger to the new arrow. The cart obeyed the board that hadn’t dried yet. She admired the quiet way he made obedience feel like the cart’s own idea. The admiration touched heat to her mouth and then left when she told it to.\n\n“Post caller,” she said. Brighlee flipped Blue to Yellow for the next weather seam and tied it with a wet‑hand knot the crews could change blind. A ringer two blocks down answered with a note that held the same softness. Place started to act like a place again.\n\nEast Gate had the worst angle. The choke perched between a vendor’s cart that had grown into a home and a pillar that belonged to people who had no interest in geometry. Tiffani chalked the rectangle on the posting slab and felt the old frustration present its familiar cleverness. She refused clever. She laid the arrows according to the map, not the wish. She set the wedge to teach the turn as a decision instead of a stunt.\n\nJall Var tried again. “Exceptions for Night,” he said. “Put a text strip with penalties.” He wanted mouths to do what maps did.\n\nTiffani pressed the quill square darker than she had at West and Center. “Map‑as‑Set is lawful,” she said to the board. She pressed a second small ridged patch in the lower left where strangers could test without wrecking the legend. “Exceptions live in your ledger. The lane runs on this.”\n\nBrighlee posted a caller post three spans back from East Gate, marked for staging. Kylar checked its tie in sequence. He touched the ring glyph and then the ridged patch with the pad of his thumb, building a ritual that made him look like a man twice his age in all the best ways.\n\nTraffic tried to be rhetorical and found it had nothing to argue with. A return runner slowed, palm on ring, eyes skimming the arrows until his body took the right retune without a thought. A woman carrying a crate held at hip level because someone had taught her that high loads become stories in the wrong ways. She slipped into the right lane, eyebrows set to focused instead of confused.\n\nThe clerks behind Jall Var traded looks and found, to their dismay, that there was less work for their mouths than they hoped. He adjusted his stance to resemble approval. He would sign later if anyone asked. If no one asked, the boards would have signed for him.\n\nCost burned harder behind Tiffani’s eyes. Words blurred for half a beat, which was fortunate because she wasn’t writing words. She sipped from the tin at her belt and held the water on her tongue until the world sharpened along its edges again. Jhace set the bar down by the Center board and came to East with no heroics and a simple, irritating understanding of when another set of hands would speed a tie. He held the board steady while she bit the legend, his knuckles at the edge of her vision like a bracket. The warmth that had been domestic inside her ribs slid to the small of her back. It did not interfere with the ridger’s line.\n\n“Three maps alike,” she said to Chloen because reality likes being invited to its own posting. Chloen put the overlays side by side on the shelf, nodded once, and stamped the quill square on each. “Standardized,” she said. She didn’t say, finally, because it wasn’t. It was just now.\n\n“Caller marks posted,” Brighlee said from the Center. She had set the small square on each post at the same height, a finger below most people’s knuckle, readable by hand in rain. Yellow showed for the seam that was coming; Blue sat behind it like a saved breath.\n\nKylar trotted the three mouths in a loop, tapping the ridged patches at each board with two fingers as if he was ringing three small bells in a pattern. The loop was not ceremony. It was a check he had taught himself.\n\nJall Var considered asking for a Latin flourish or its local cousin on the lower margin. He found no purchase and hung a satisfied face on his skull to see if it would attach to the outcome. Tiffani didn’t feed the attempt with so much as a look. She had ink to press and a last correction to make at the East Gate where the wedge wanted to lie about its angle.\n\nShe corrected it. The room felt the correction like a person cracking a knuckle he didn’t know was stiff. The next cart took the turn with a tire that did not shriek; the sound fell from the lane like a dropped habit.\n\n“Board plates match,” Chloen said, laying the three small overlay tiles on the shelf in a row. She pointed once at the arrow tooth and then at the chalk on the stones under each. “West, Center, East: the same story.”\n\nTiffani stood back until all three boards fit in her head at once, eyes blurring to spare their nerves and then pulling sharp. The three maps said the same thing in the same language. The boards made the people behave like citizens of a single corridor instead of ambassadors from their personal weather.\n\nShe felt the cost finally push through the discipline: the fine ache at the outer corners of her eyes, the grit that wasn’t grit but a muscle asking not to be used for five minutes. She put her thumb to the ridged patch at Center and let the texture’s tiny stubbornness give her own back. She stole half a breath to notice Jhace knotting a tie at East with clean wrists, the way he does when his mouth is busy being not in the way. Heat rose and then settled like a good veil. She put it on a hook in her chest for later.\n\n“Done,” she said, mostly to the boards, partly to the night.\n\nBrighlee made the last posting: a small caller note tied under each map, icon for **one hand on home** printed beside a bell mark to remind night crews that time and place still exist when the lamps go gloomy. Kylar rubbed the ring glyph under the West board with his whole palm and grinned at no one when the paint shone back more honestly.\n\nTiffani pressed the quill square one last time on East for luck she didn’t admit to believing in. She pressed the ridger across the legend so the board would tell on itself if someone tried to lift it. She tied the lower left corner again because a corner is where lies start.\n\nThe bell down‑lane gave its seam. Night crews paused and then chose the lanes the boards had already given them. The corridor lowered in pitch. Steam made polite ribbons. The clerk trio found they were supernumerary and wandered off to look like inspectors somewhere else.\n\nShe closed the kit and slid it under her coat. She touched two fingertips to the Center board’s ridged patch and then put those fingers to her temple, a private negotiation with the ache there. The world honored the request and sharpened a fraction.\n\nOn the corridor wall, three Map‑as‑Set boards hung at West, Center, and East, identical in their language: arrows meeting at a tooth, a ring at waist height, a wedge set honest, bell pairs along the bottom, quill squares biting dark. At each caller post, a small square hung at the same height, marked and ready. In the slick light, the three maps read like a single sentence, and the lane obeyed without anyone needing to be convincing."}

[ASSISTANT]
Chapter 12 is up. Three lanes, one language, steward sidelined, and the heat between them nudged forward without hijacking the job.

{"name":"Novella 2 — Chapter 13: Escort Doctrine","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 13: Escort Doctrine\n\nJhace came to the West Mouth with the new board under his arm and a patience that had already spent half its coins. Morning steam lay low, mixing with the cold like breath on iron. A small procession stacked at the choke: a porter line with crates, a midwife with a wrapped basket, two Weights clerks carrying a tray under oilcloth, and a steward’s nephew trying to wear his uncle’s ledger like a badge. Everyone wanted to be an exception. The corridor wanted something else.\n\n“Caller post live,” Brighlee said from his elbow. She had the square ready to flip and ties between her fingers. Kylar hovered at ring height like a habit.\n\nJhace lifted the board to the wall shelf and chalked the rectangle. Action inside the minute or the lane invents its own doctrine. He opened the kit. Plates sat waiting: ring glyph, arrows, bell bars, small hands, caller icon. He checked the ink’s drag with a thumb. Good weight. Rain wasn’t here to lie yet.\n\nHe set the ring glyph first, large and honest at waist height, the circle thickened to teach touch. This board would not be read so much as handled. He measured from his belt to the board and set the paint deep so the ridger would have ink to bite. He stamped the Conclave quill square top right, not to ask permission but to make arguing feel expensive.\n\nUnder the ring he placed a line of plates that made a sentence: a small hand icon meeting the ring, an arrow showing the lane’s home edge, a caller glyph at the post, bell marks in two short bars, a second hand marking tail. Icon only. The text, if anyone asked, would be bodies.\n\n“Escort Doctrine,” he said to the wall, to the tools, to his ribs. “One hand on home, caller sets windows, lead and tail keep the shape. Crowd is material.” The last phrase he had lifted from Tiffani when she said it like a rule and not a metaphor. He let it sit in his mouth because it fit the metal and the morning.\n\nJall Var’s nephew tried for his line. “Steward’s parcel needs immediate pass.” He held the ledger so its corner pointed like an arrow at someone else’s work.\n\nJhace tipped his chin at the ring glyph without looking at the boy. “Hand on home,” he said. “Your parcel learns to be a person.” He pointed to the caller post. “Wait for window.” He kept his voice even. Patience tax due; pay it with smaller coins.\n\nHe pressed the ridger into the legend strip. The teeth sang against ink, leaving a ridge a thumb could audit even with cold hands. He pressed a second patch low, crosshatched for strangers. Kylar threaded ties through the corners and left them loose‑tight the way Jhace liked so the board could be re‑centered without a fight.\n\n“Make it live,” he said. Brighlee flipped the caller square from Blue to Yellow to announce a window coming. The ringer down‑lane gave back two clean notes as if they had rehearsed.\n\nHe moved to the lane and set the first escort with what was in front of him. “Porters first,” he said. “Midwife in the pocket behind lead. Weights clerks tail.” He placed bodies with a finger, not a command. He tapped the ring glyph at the board with his knuckle and then showed the same height on the air like drawing a line on a pane.\n\nKylar took the lead. He placed his left hand to the ring painted on the wall, then slid that height to the home edge of the lane and kept it there, palm brushing the painted stone as if reading it. The porters mirrored him along the line. The midwife settled behind the second shoulder and put her free hand on the ring with a breath he could hear. The Weights clerks’ tray balanced when their inner wrists touched the ring height without crowding it. It looked like nothing much from a distance and like intelligence at arm’s length.\n\nJhace lifted the rod. Two short taps. Tap tap. Brighlee raised two fingers to the bell. Yellow held steady. The escort moved on the window, not through it, occupying the shape the board had promised them. The crowd leaned to invent a shortcut and found their bodies already had the better idea because their palms were busy being eyes.\n\nHe walked alongside, not in front. A man tried to wedge a hand‑barrow between midwife and tail because gaps ask to be filled. Jhace reached out with his knuckles and drew a small arc in the air at ring height. The man’s hand, itself a student, found the ring and then found the home edge and backed out. No talk. The ring did the talking.\n\nTiffani arrived from Center Notch with a coil of spare ties and a veil roller she’d refused to abandon even for corridor work. She put a palm to the ring glyph as she passed, like a person greeting a tool. He saw her without looking and felt the small heat step toward his chest and stand there, not asking to be fed, only saying yes I’m here. He boxed it and set it on a shelf where he could see it and not trip over it. Competence keeps its own room warm.\n\n“Second escort ready,” Brighlee said. She had two fingers against the caller square and her eyes on the ringer. Kylar returned at a trot, touched the ring, and took the tail this time so muscles learned both directions. The steward’s nephew hovered, ledger lifted and lowering in defeat. He put his hand to the ring and looked surprised to find the impulse didn’t kill him.\n\nJall Var himself came later, ledger at the precise angle that avoided the rain that wasn’t falling yet. “Exceptions,” he said. “Officials ahead of bulk.” He said it like the board had asked for flourish.\n\nJhace put his thumb on the ridged patch and let the texture slow the blood in his wrists. “Exceptions break shape,” he said. He touched the plates in sequence with the rod’s cap, ticking like a quiet bell: ring, hand, caller, bell pair, tail hand. “If shape breaks, the lane breaks. Post is law.” He did not look away from the board to answer a mouth. He looked at the crowd that obeyed a posted shape and let that be the argument.\n\nHe built the third escort the way he wished someone had built him the first time he held a bar and made a street remember how to move. Lead was a runner with good knees; tail was a vendor who knew his weight. He set them with hands, set them with the ring, set them with the idea that people are better when their palms are busy being part of a surface.\n\nCost showed in small scuffs. His patience frayed at the edges as the nephew tried new pronunciations of exception. Jhace breathed four and six until the fray reseated. His voice wanted to lift; he didn’t let it. He paid the tax by repeating the rod‑cap tick along the plates: ring, hand, caller, bell, hand. It steadied him as much as it taught.\n\nHe added one last piece to the board. A small plate for roles along the bottom edge: **lead**, **mid**, **tail** in icon only, little hands at ring height on each. He pressed the ridger again across the lower legend so the patch would tell if lifted. He stamped the quill darker, because Jall Var’s eyes liked ink he could name later as his ally.\n\nThe next window laid down. Tap tap. The third escort moved. A woman at the rear put her palm to the ring and kept it there even after she passed the choke, as if the doctrine might travel with her down the lane. It would. That was the point.\n\nTiffani posted herself at the far mouth and mirrored the ring glyph on the low pillar that had been trying not to be part of a corridor for three generations. She set the circle at the same height, wiped rain that wasn’t there yet, and looked back across the lane along the line of ring to ring. He met her eyes by accident. Heat rose, cleaner than earlier, like a pan just off the fire. He didn’t move toward it; he let it brighten the work in front of him.\n\nValaryn arrived long enough to hang a small **rest** slip on the clinic nook near the mouth, return times adjusted to windows so escorts could release and still get water. He nodded at her without words and felt the doctrine settle into other institutions where it belonged.\n\nThe steward’s nephew tried one last time with a different logic. “If I touch the ring, do I get to go first?”\n\n“Touch the ring to go correctly,” Jhace said. “First belongs to the job.” The boy set his palm to the paint with a flinch that became curiosity. He kept it there until the caller gave the pairs. He went when the window said, and nothing in his body complained.\n\nBy the fourth run the doctrine had collected enough behavior to be its own enforcement. Hands found the ring without looking. People staged themselves at the caller post instead of pooling at its base. The home edge turned from a concept into something you could lean on with your hip without rolling into anyone else’s life.\n\nHe walked the edge and checked the board’s ties. He pressed a second ridged patch at the lower left because corners are where thieves test the world. He added a small ring glyph on the pillar opposite, waist height again, so escorts could touch with either hand and not twist themselves into bad shapes.\n\nThe ringer sent a clean note across the seam. Brighlee flipped Yellow back to Blue. Kylar ran the edge touching ring glyphs with two fingers as if he were checking pulse points. Tiffani, done with the far ring, stood at the mouth like a pane that had been annealed properly. He let himself look that one second longer than the work required. Heat moved, then took its place in the structure the way a brace does when the angle’s right.\n\nJall Var didn’t sign anything. He nodded in a way that made it look like he had. Fine. The board had signed enough.\n\nJhace closed the kit and set his own palm to the ring for no one’s benefit. The paint felt cool, the ridger’s small teeth left their story in his skin. The lane’s pitch had dropped to workable. The doctrine, posted, had become a surface. He stepped back until the whole frame fit: board with ring and hand plates, caller post with square ready, bell marks set, bodies moving on windows like water choosing a bed.\n\nHe pressed the rod’s cap to the legend strip one last time to teach his bones the board was real. Then he watched the next escort go through, hands at ring height each, lead and tail in quiet conversation with a surface that didn’t talk back.\n\nOn the posting wall at West Mouth, the Escort Doctrine board hung square: ring glyph bold at waist height, small hand plates marching in a line, caller and bell marks tight, quill square biting the corner, two ridged patches for fingers to prove. Along the stone at the choke, the ring paint shone. Many palms had already polished it bright."}

{"name":"Novella 2 — Chapter 14: Posting Rite (Rain)","type":"document","content":"# Chapter 14: Posting Rite (Rain)\n\nTiffani carried the kit under her coat like a warm brick. Rain came at a slant that erased edges and turned Narrowvale into a set of wet planes. The posting walls sweated water; bell housings drummed. This was the rite: tempermark every working board for weather, then sweep for the last counterfeit that had learned to pretend a ridge.\n\nKylar jogged ahead with the cord and the small hammer. Brighlee moved opposite with ties and a strip of waxed cloth held like a roof for tools. Jhace was already at Center Notch with a shoulder under a board, giving it the dry his coat could afford. He caught Tiffani’s eye and set a palm to the edge so she could step into his shelter without asking. Heat moved under her collarbone the way a lamp lifts one wick. She filed it in the clean cupboard labeled later.\n\nAction first. She opened the kit, checked the ink’s drag with a thumb. Good pull. She slid out the ridger, its teeth a fine comb. The first board was West Mouth. She wiped rain with her sleeve and pressed the ridger across the legend strip, steady heel of palm, teeth biting the tall ink. Raised ridge. She pressed again at the test patch she’d cut for the rite, crosshatch for public thumbs.\n\n“Conclave square,” Brighlee said. Tiffani set the quill stamp top right, the wood changing tone under her hand. Water beaded and wouldn’t settle. Good.\n\nEast Gate next. The rain found seams and spoke through them. She bit the legend, pressed the droplet icon small beside the quill to mark weather‑ready status. The plate left its honest curve. Kylar checked the ties and ran his thumb across the patch like a man learning his letters at speed.\n\nCenter Notch was already half covered by Jhace’s coat. He leaned in so she could work, their shoulders touching under the improvised roof. His breath was warm and even; hers wanted to match. She pressed the ridger and felt the teeth hum through the ink into the wood. Ridge stood. She stamped the quill and the droplet again and slid the tool back into its cloth.\n\nThey moved to the sweep. Counterfeits like edges and early morning. Rain makes ink tell on itself. Tiffani started south to north. She pressed her thumb to every ridged patch in reach, not trusting eyes. Real ridges push back even when cold. Flat lies pretend to be proud.\n\nKylar stopped at a small caution plate that hadn’t been there yesterday. Good layout, wedge upstream, arrows clean. A ridged patch lived at the lower corner. He tested it and nodded, the way a boy hungry to be helpful nods. “Has bite,” he said.\n\nTiffani didn’t answer. She looked past the patch to the field. Ink sheen was a hair too wet for this rain. She rubbed the pad of her thumb, then set it to the ridge and held it there through a count.\n\nThe ridge softened.\n\nThe pad slid.\n\nHer thumb came away with a faint gray smear.\n\n“Wax,” she said, soft. The forger had borrowed texture from weather instead of fighting it. Under dry, the ridge read. Under rain, it died to flat. Clever in the way cheap is clever.\n\nKylar flushed, not for being wrong but for nearly posting belief to the crowd. He recoiled as if he’d touched a stove. Tiffani took his wrist, anchoring without blame. “We add the wet test,” she said. “On all of them.” He nodded and set the hammer down to free both hands for work that mattered.\n\nBrighlee flowed sideways to make a small wall between the counterfeit and eyes that might turn to rumor. Jhace stepped behind Tiffani and lifted his coat again so she could work without the rain lying. His shoulder brushed hers; her heat rose a notch and then steadied into the same box labeled later, right beside the tool oil.\n\nShe curled the counterfeit’s corner and looked at the back. Adhesive spread cheap and thin. The paper fiber was too fine. The ink at the field had bled into itself in a way good ink doesn’t. She pressed the ridger into the waxed patch; the teeth scored the wax like a crumble, left no ridge. She said nothing to the crowd; she spoke directly to the board.\n\n“Pulling,” she said. She lifted the lie carefully and tore it across the field so no one could stick it back up and call it a misunderstanding. The tear left a gray edge that rain turned to soup. She set the piece into the burn tray Kylar held open and tapped a pinch of drying sand over it so the mess didn’t wander. Brighlee posted a small **warning** square in its place with the quill bite and a fresh ridged patch.\n\n“Rite continues,” Tiffani said, not to reassure anyone, only to set sequence. “Wet test on all posts.” Kylar’s nod arrived like a small vow.\n\nThey moved fast. At each board Tiffani pressed the ridger and then ran water from her cupped palm over the patch until drops stood and walked. She set her thumb and held for a count of three. Real ridges grow clearer under water; lies become grease. The team learned the new ritual so quickly it looked like they’d always known it.\n\nFailure tried a second angle. On a board at the far edge of East Gate, her first bite held, but when she poured water the ink lifted in a thin skin. Not counterfeit—bad stock. She felt the breath leave her chest as a sigh she hadn’t earned. Her hand shook a sliver from fatigue and irritation. Jhace slid a palm to the board’s far side to brace without speaking and gave her the dry edge of his coat again. She steadied. “Ink swap,” she said. Brighlee had the backup pot ready. Tiffani checked drag this time twice, then pressed a new ridge over the old field and watched the water bead and honor it.\n\nJall Var arrived with damp anger tucked under a neutral face. “You see?” he tried. “Icons invite forgery.” He looked like a man who wanted to sell text as if rain could read.\n\nTiffani lifted the burn tray with the counterfeit scrap and tipped it to show the gray smear without theater. “Forgeries invite removal,” she said. She set the tray back and went to the next board. “We post for weather; the ridge stands.” She didn’t give him a place to wedge the word exception.\n\nThe rain thickened and then settled to an honest pour. Brighlee posted a small **Rite Live** square at each mouth with a droplet plate over a quill, so people would understand why fingers touched ridges all morning. Kylar moved ahead, tapping patches with two fingers and then pouring a splash from a tin so strangers could watch the test without needing instruction. The gesture taught itself.\n\nThey reached the north wall. One last board. It held a map that belonged to yesterday and a wedge angle that encouraged cheerfully suicidal turns. The ridged patch at its lower corner looked good even under rain. Tiffani heard the part of herself that wanted to be done. She put a knee on that part and tested anyway. Thumb down. Count. The ridge held. Good.\n\nShe pressed the ridger anew, stamped the quill and the droplet, then added a second crosshatch patch beside the legend with a tiny droplet icon next to it, a sanctioned place to rub wet. “Public patch,” she said. “If it goes flat, shout.” She didn’t mean voices. She meant the kind of shout the board does when it tells the truth.\n\nKylar untied the counterfeit warning square and replaced it with the corrected card on the mid‑wall. Brighlee set ties so wet hands could adjust without tearing. Jhace stayed at her side under the shared pocket of dry until she stepped back. The warmth between them wanted to be more than warmth. She let it be warmth anyway and felt her breath even into the working pace they’d kept all season.\n\n“Done?” Brighlee asked.\n\n“Two checks,” Tiffani said. She walked the three mouths and pressed her thumb to every patch again, this time letting rain fall clean without her hand. Ridge stood on each. Water beaded and ran the way water should run: down along the painted wood, off the lower edge, back to the stones where drains had been taught their jobs long before she was born.\n\nCrowd behavior shifted. People began to perform the wet test for themselves on the crosshatch, grinning when the texture pushed back. A boy who had been taught to doubt everything put two thumbs to two patches and looked like a person holding a secret he would parade without cruelty. Jall Var couldn’t find a place to sell fear.\n\nTiffani took one last breath under the small pocket of dry they’d built between coat and board. She could smell metal and ink and the wet wool of Jhace’s sleeve. The heat the day had made stayed where it was, steady and possible. She closed the kit and handed Kylar the ridger to wipe because he liked that job more than he said.\n\nThey returned to West Mouth for the posting proper. She set the final stamp: a tiny droplet plate tucked beside the quill on each board’s top right. **Rite complete** lived as a shape, not words. She pressed the ridger along each legend strip once more, shallow and even, an underlining you feel instead of read.\n\nThe rain eased to a civilized line. The last counterfeit sat blackened in the burn tray, wax ridge collapsed into a shine that would fool nobody twice. The boards along the lane wore their weather marks.\n\nOn the posted boards, water beaded and ran off without warping. At each legend, the Tempermark ridges stood proud under wet thumbs, crosshatch patches darker from honest use, the small droplet icon set beside the quill like a promise kept."}

[ASSISTANT]
Chapter 14 is posted. The rain rite bit back: a wax-ridge fake almost slid through, one ink pot tried to betray them, and they answered with a new wet test and weather marks that actually hold. Also, yes—the warmth between them is no longer shy, it’s disciplined.
